# NEG -- SMRs -- BBEJM 

# Case Neg

## Solvency/Uniqueness

### Solvency\-\--Adoption Turn\-\--1NC

#### The AFF empirically [tarnishes]{.underline} commercial adoption of SMRs.

Michael J. **Ford et al. '18**, French Environmental Fellow at the
Harvard University Center for the Environment, former nuclear-trained
officer in the US Navy, Ahmed Abdulla, research scientist at the
University of California, Adjunct Assistant Professor @ Carnegie Mellon
University, M. Granger Morgan, Hamerschlag University Professor of
Engineering at Carnegie Mellon University, Issues, "Nuclear Power Needs
Leadership, but Not from the Military",
https://issues.org/nuclear-power-needs-leadership-but-not-from-the-military/,
lmk

Practical arguments

[[If DoD]{.mark} were to consider [acquiring]{.mark} a fleet of
[SMRs]{.mark}]{.underline}, [it would start by]{.underline} assessing
its need for them and [identifying]{.underline} the [key mission
performance parameters]{.underline} [they would need to
satisfy]{.underline}. DoD manages the development of new programs
through its Joint Capabilities Integration and Development System, which
starts by identifying either new threats or gaps in current
capabilities. [Assuming a valid gap is identified]{.underline}, the
[process]{.underline} then [moves through material
solutions]{.underline} analysis---essentially [looking at all possible
alternatives]{.underline} to fill that gap. [[There is little
evidence]{.mark} that the [SMR designs that would emerge]{.mark} from
this]{.underline} analytic [process [would be appropriate for]{.mark}
wide [commercial deployment]{.mark}]{.underline}. In fact,
[most]{.underline} of the [[evidence suggests]{.mark} **quite** [the
**opposite**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

The [[operational requirements]{.mark} and performance standards that
would be critical [in a]{.mark} v[SMR]{.mark} [slated for use in forward
operating bases would]{.mark}]{.underline} most likely
[[yield]{.underline} [a very small reactor]{.underline}]{.mark}
(nominally less than 10 megawatts of electricity), [[blunting]{.mark}
its broad **[commercial desirability]{.mark}**]{.underline}. In addition
to their small size, these [reactors would likely be developed
with]{.underline} other [[characteristics]{.mark} that may [prove
**troublesome** for]{.mark} commercial [**industry** or **regulatory
bodies**]{.mark}]{.underline}. First, [they would need to have a **long
core** life]{.underline}, which means they would require fuel
"**[enriched]{.underline}**" with **[uranium-235]{.underline}** to a
higher level than used in commercial reactors. Second, [they would have
to be transportable and thus rugged]{.underline}. [Third]{.underline},
for [variants]{.underline} that are [small enough to be
deployed]{.underline} [in]{.underline} remote or forward operating
[bases]{.underline}, the [design [might]{.mark} have to [employ very
**modest** **containment structures**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Fourth, they
would be designed to absorb shocks and to continue operating in spite of
these. Fifth, control [systems would]{.underline} most likely [have to
be automated to a great degree]{.underline}, because it costs money to
train personnel, and [[DoD would **not look kindly** on SMRs that
turn]{.mark} forward operating [**bases** into **superbly guarded power
plants**]{.mark}]{.underline} that have more plant operators than
warfighters.

Though SMRs designed to provide power to US military bases could be
larger, [it is likely that [DoD]{.mark}]{.underline}
[[would]{.underline}]{.mark} still [[want **substantially different**
tech]{.mark}nical [specifications from]{.mark} those of **[commercial
reactors]{.mark}**]{.underline}. As with vSMR variants, sound arguments
can be made for more highly enriched cores to lengthen refueling
intervals, greater ruggedness to support defense and security
resilience, and robust standalone control systems to limit vulnerability
to cyber and physical risks.

The characteristics outlined above would most likely be endorsed by many
reactor designers, and [[some]{.underline}]{.mark} experts [have [called
for DoD to be an early leader]{.mark} to ensure technical
specifications]{.underline} such as these [are tailored to meet unique
DoD requirements]{.underline}. [[But it is difficult to overstate how
**politically unpalatable**]{.mark} some]{.underline} of them [[would
be]{.mark} to the Nuclear Regulatory Commission]{.underline}. This is
especially true of any move toward higher fuel enrichment. Should they
be incorporated into one system, [we have [great difficulty
envisioning]{.mark} a [license]{.mark} being granted [without
**institutional reforms**]{.mark} so deep that they would be sufficient
to enhance nuclear power's prospects on their own, [weakening]{.mark}
the [case for the **military's involvement**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Of
course, since DoD regulates its own reactors, it would not have to deal
with these institutional constraints. However, [the argument that [its
role as **first mover**]{.mark} could accelerate the commercial
licensing process [is **not**]{.mark}]{.underline} very
**[[compelling]{.underline}]{.mark}**.

Moreover, [[any SMR fit for DoD's needs would]{.underline}]{.mark}
likely [[be **too expensive** for a commercial utility to
deploy]{.underline}]{.mark}. As noted above, [the [same
**requirements**]{.mark} and standards on [which **DoD would
insist**]{.mark} might [render commercial variants **economically
uncompetitive**]{.mark}]{.underline} in most of today's markets. [In
fact]{.underline}, the [military variant itself might not be deployed
widely]{.underline}: [even if]{.underline} the [political will did
materialize]{.underline}, and substantial appropriations were
forthcoming, and industry eagerly participated, [the [likelihood of
**successful project** execution is still
**modest**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Given the [long history of cost
overruns and waste in defense acquisition]{.underline},
[the]{.underline} [[**empirical record** is]{.mark} now [so robust that
this statement is]{.mark} almost **[axiomatic]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

### Solvency\-\--Adoption Turn\-\--2NC

#### The AFF constructs a [military-industrial complex]{.underline} entirely reliant on the DoD

Michael J. **Ford et al. '18**, French Environmental Fellow at the
Harvard University Center for the Environment, former nuclear-trained
officer in the US Navy, Ahmed Abdulla, research scientist at the
University of California, Adjunct Assistant Professor @ Carnegie Mellon
University, M. Granger Morgan, Hamerschlag University Professor of
Engineering at Carnegie Mellon University, Issues, "Nuclear Power Needs
Leadership, but Not from the Military",
https://issues.org/nuclear-power-needs-leadership-but-not-from-the-military/,
lmk

[[Should]{.underline}]{.mark} a fleet of [[SMRs]{.mark} developed by DoD
[somehow]{.mark}]{.underline} [[succeed]{.mark} in]{.underline}
fulfilling its ([poorly defined]{.underline}) [mission]{.underline}, and
should it succeed in spawning a following within Congress, [another
prosaic question]{.underline}---that [of **intellectual property**
ownership and **program management**]{.underline}---[would
emerge]{.underline}. Once DoD had deployed several dozen SMRs, and once
the infrastructure that supports these SMRs was developed, even
following a "fee for service" model, the [military might consider
bringing the entire enterprise in-house]{.underline}. This would
effectively create another Naval Reactors program and [would surely
**engender strong opposition**]{.underline}. Given the [contentious
nature of budget battles across the services]{.underline}, the [military
does not need **additional fiefdoms**]{.underline}. The creation of
[Naval Reactors was]{.underline} initially [**highly controversial**,
and [any SMR fleet]{.mark} that relied extensively on DoD]{.underline}
development and [[deployment would **undoubtedly** create
another]{.mark} such **[mini-empire]{.mark}**]{.underline}. For such a
[program]{.underline} not to [[devolve into a
**fiasco**]{.underline}]{.mark}, a similarly vigorous level of project
management, situational awareness, and [**political lobbying** would be
**required**]{.underline}. Military organizations that have been created
to address more critical warfighting needs, such as the Joint Improvised
Explosive Device Defeat Organization, grew far beyond their original
mandate and expected scope. [[If commercial development **lags** or
**fails**]{.mark} to materialize]{.underline}, there is also the [[very
real possibility of]{.underline}]{.mark} developing another [[industrial
base]{.mark} component]{.underline} that is [[**entirely reliant** on
the **military**]{.mark} for support]{.underline}, much like
shipbuilding. [Parts]{.underline} of the military-industrial complex
[already [resemble a **monopsony**]{.mark}]{.underline}, with programs
maintained to support the nation's industrial base despite often-limited
warfighting impact: the controversy surrounding the [USS Zumwalt-class
guided-missile destroyer]{.underline}, which many critics have [called a
**boondoggle**, serves as a prime example]{.underline}.

#### Maintaining a [firewall]{.underline} between [civilian]{.underline} and [military]{.underline} nuclear power is key to [industry success]{.underline} and [military superiority]{.underline}. Erosion [collapses]{.underline} the industry AND kills US credibility in norms setting.

Michael J. **Ford et al. '18**, French Environmental Fellow at the
Harvard University Center for the Environment, former nuclear-trained
officer in the US Navy, Ahmed Abdulla, research scientist at the
University of California, Adjunct Assistant Professor @ Carnegie Mellon
University, M. Granger Morgan, Hamerschlag University Professor of
Engineering at Carnegie Mellon University, Issues, "Nuclear Power Needs
Leadership, but Not from the Military",
https://issues.org/nuclear-power-needs-leadership-but-not-from-the-military/,
lmk

Often, both **[advocates]{.underline}** and critics [of nuclear power
note that the [**firebreak** between]{.mark} the [**civilian** and
**military nuclear programs**]{.mark} is artificial]{.underline}. But
[no matter how]{.underline} [imprecise or imperfect, this firebreak [has
**substantial normative** value]{.mark}]{.underline}. It
[[ensures]{.underline}]{.mark} that [[**power** and **weapons** remain
**separate**]{.mark} enough [for **evolutions**]{.mark} in the latter
not [to **compromise** or **cloud the future** of the
former]{.mark}]{.underline}. [Maintaining [this separation is]{.mark}
perhaps the easiest way to **[guarantee]{.mark}** that
[US]{.mark}]{.underline}-made [**[SMRs]{.mark}** [are]{.mark}
**[exportable]{.mark}**]{.underline} [[while ensuring]{.mark} that
qualitative [US **military superiority**]{.mark}]{.underline} in reactor
development is maintained. Also, [[if the boundary were
**eroded**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[it would make it]{.mark} far more
[**difficult** for the]{.mark}]{.underline}
[[U]{.underline}]{.mark}nited [[S]{.underline}]{.mark}tates [[to
**restore** its **standing** with respect to **international controls**
on]{.underline}]{.mark} the development and use of civilian [[nuclear
power]{.underline}]{.mark} and fuel cycles.

#### The AFF [kills]{.underline} credibility of the technology -- amounts to an [admission of failure]{.underline}. Plan doesn't solve\-\--problems are fundamentally [political]{.underline} AND [economic]{.underline}.

Michael J. **Ford et al. '18**, French Environmental Fellow at the
Harvard University Center for the Environment, former nuclear-trained
officer in the US Navy, Ahmed Abdulla, research scientist at the
University of California, Adjunct Assistant Professor @ Carnegie Mellon

Engineering at Carnegie Mellon University, Issues, "Nuclear Power Needs
Leadership, but Not from the Military",
https://issues.org/nuclear-power-needs-leadership-but-not-from-the-military/,
lmk

Finally, [[adopting this model would **amount** to **an admission of
failure** on the]{.mark} nuclear [industry's part]{.mark}]{.underline}.
[Defaulting to]{.underline} the **[national security
argument]{.underline}** in an effort [to [**salvage** the **commercial
nuclear** ]{.mark}**industry** **[concedes]{.mark}** the [**failure**
of]{.mark} the technical and economic arguments in favor of **[the
tech]{.mark}**nology]{.underline}. The [[nuclear]{.mark} energy
enterprise [is at a **critical juncture**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [its
[problems are **unlikely** to **abate**]{.mark}]{.underline} in the near
future, [and [most]{.mark}]{.underline} of these [[are **fundamentally
economic** and **political**]{.underline}]{.mark}. The deregulation of
the electricity markets has yielded utilities that focus on short-term
profits and neglect long-term, structural challenges. [Every
[lender]{.mark}]{.underline} with the assets to finance generation
expansion [is similarly [preoccupied with **short-term
profits**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Moreover, [US [**energy markets** remain
**warped**]{.mark}]{.underline}: where [[policies]{.mark}
favor]{.underline} [low-carbon power]{.underline}, they [**often
[exclude]{.mark}** [nuclear]{.mark} technologies]{.underline}. These are
profound problems that deserve attention, and fixing them will benefit
nuclear power, the nation, and the climate.

### Solvency\-\--No Adoption\-\--1NC

#### No SMR adoption\-\--they're [unproven]{.underline}, [unprofitable]{.underline}, and are [heavily regulated]{.underline}.

**Gaster '25** \[Robin; April 14; PhD in Economics from UC Berkeley,
president of Incumentrics Inc, visiting scholar at George Washington
University, "Small Modular Reactors: A Realist Approach to the Future of
Nuclear Power,"
https://itif.org/publications/2025/04/14/small-modular-reactors-a-realist-approach-to-the-future-of-nuclear-power/\]

Of course, first-of-a-kind [[SMRs]{.underline}]{.mark} are [[going to be
**expensive**]{.underline}]{.mark}. They will [likely [cost more per
megawatt]{.mark}]{.underline} hour (MWh) than [**existing** large
reactors]{.underline}, and [**certainly** more [than **competing
fuels**]{.mark}]{.underline} (solar, wind, and natural gas). Expensive
new technologies that will [take a **decade** or more]{.underline} to
reach scale (if they ever do) are very high risk, and SMRs face four
distinct kinds of risk:

[Technology risks]{.underline}. Until the reactors are up and running,
[we [won't know]{.mark}]{.underline} whether in practice [[they
meet]{.mark} the [**anticipated** specs]{.mark}]{.underline}. Because we
[don't have operational **experience**]{.underline} yet,
[[SMR]{.underline}]{.mark} technology [[could fail]{.mark} to
translate]{.underline} from the drawing board on pilot projects to full
commercial operation in multiple ways---they [could [generate **less**
power]{.mark}]{.underline}, use [[more **fuel**]{.underline}]{.mark},
require [[more **downtime**]{.underline}]{.mark}; there is a [**long
list** of things that could go wrong]{.underline}.

[Market risks]{.underline}. [SMRs]{.underline} as a
[[business]{.underline}]{.mark} face [[risks]{.mark} on
[both]{.mark}]{.underline} the **[[supply]{.underline}]{.mark}** side
[[and]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[**demand** side]{.underline}]{.mark}.
Aside from the technical risks, SMR companies may find that the
[[competition]{.underline}]{.mark} is [[more **intense** or
**effective**]{.mark} than anticipated]{.underline}; that some
[assumptions about]{.underline} their [supply chain are
wrong]{.underline}; or even that key [[patents don't hold
up]{.underline}]{.mark}. And of course---as with large
reactors---companies may simply find that [producing and
siting]{.underline} SMRs is much [more expensive]{.underline} than
expected, or takes much longer, or that interest rates shift sharply
upward, or indeed that inflation suddenly hits key inputs. On the demand
side, it may be that [[expected markets]{.underline}]{.mark} simply
[[don't materialize]{.underline}]{.mark}, or that there is [no market
for SMR energy]{.underline} at the price it needs to charge. It's hard
to predict energy markets a decade out.

[Regulatory risks]{.underline}. SMRs [[must
navigate]{.underline}]{.mark} multiple **[[layers of
regulation]{.underline}]{.mark}**. They [[need]{.mark} to
get]{.underline} their [[designs **certified**]{.underline}]{.mark} for
safety (in the United States, [by the]{.underline} Nuclear Regulatory
Commission ([NRC]{.underline})). They need to get NRC safety [approval
for]{.underline} their [operating plan for a specific site]{.underline}.
They then need to get site approval via the National Environmental
Protection Act (NEPA) process to ensure that environmental issues have
been addressed. That may well also involve a defense against NIMBY ("not
in my backyard") lawsuits. And as SMRs will need to scale globally, they
will [also [need to address regulators in **other
countries**]{.mark}.]{.underline}

#### There are a [litany]{.underline} of more profitable energy sources.

**Gaster '25** \[Robin; April 14; PhD in Economics from UC Berkeley,
president of Incumentrics Inc, visiting scholar at George Washington
University, "Small Modular Reactors: A Realist Approach to the Future of
Nuclear Power,"
https://itif.org/publications/2025/04/14/small-modular-reactors-a-realist-approach-to-the-future-of-nuclear-power/\]

**[Beyond costs]{.underline}**, competitive energy
[technologies]{.underline} offer a [**[different]{.mark}** supply-side
[challenge]{.mark}]{.underline}. In the United States, that
[competition]{.underline} comes from [multiple directions:]{.underline}

**[[Gas]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Natural gas is [[by
far]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[cheapest source]{.mark} of]{.underline}
firm 24/7 [power]{.underline} in the United States. It's [relatively
[**quick** and **simple** to build]{.mark}]{.underline} more CCGT and
peaker plants, and [natural gas is almost **ubiquitous**]{.underline}.
However, this is largely a U.S. phenomenon. Europe is suffering because
gas is expensive and it is largely dependent on flows from Russia. The
latter may be mitigated by more liquefied natural gas imports,
especially from the United States, but those won't be cheap.

**[Coal]{.underline}**. While [[coal is **cheap**]{.underline}]{.mark},
it is clearly on the way out---but only in the United States and perhaps
Europe. [Germany]{.underline} is [turning to coal]{.underline} for
dispatchable power when its wind-based generation fails. In
[India]{.underline} and [China]{.underline}, coal [use is [growing
**rapidly**]{.mark}]{.underline}, and remains the [[**cheapest** form
of]{.mark} [energy]{.mark}]{.underline}---and hence the [most formidable
competitor]{.underline}---in much of the developing world.

[**[Large]{.mark}** nuclear [reactors]{.mark}]{.underline}. It's
possible that, in the end, [[SMRs won't have]{.underline}]{.mark}
significant [[cost advantages]{.underline}]{.mark} over large reactors.
Big reactors do have economies of scale [[in site
**construction**]{.mark} and [operations]{.mark}]{.underline}. Those
could perhaps [more than balance out reactor cost]{.underline}
advantage[s]{.underline} for SMRs, even if cheaper factory-built
reactors become a reality.

Other clean energy. **[[Wind]{.underline}]{.mark}** and
**[[solar]{.underline}]{.mark}** are widely expected to [[eventually
**dominate**]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[energy]{.mark}
matrix]{.underline}. While their total system costs must include
[[necessary storage]{.underline}]{.mark} and [often [new
transmission]{.mark}]{.underline} (both costs that are often ignored by
wind and solar enthusiasts), [energy]{.underline} from wind and solar
[is]{.underline} cheap [now]{.underline}, and [[getting
**cheaper**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

**[New technologies]{.underline}**. There are [[promising new
technologies]{.underline}]{.mark} on the horizon [that [could provide
firm]{.mark}]{.underline} dispatchable [[energy]{.underline}]{.mark},
notably **[[geothermal]{.underline}]{.mark}** and
**[[fusion]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Geothermal could become [important
within a decade]{.underline}, if the initial results from Fervo prove
out.

Transmission. If out-of-area energy from across the grid became cheaper
and more available, the need for localized firm power would decline,
reducing the market incentives for SMRs.

**[[Batteries]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Similarly, continued [**rapid**
[cost declines]{.mark}]{.underline} and [**capacity growth** for
batteries]{.underline}---perhaps especially in combination with improved
transmission---might [also [make SMRs **less**
competitive]{.mark}]{.underline}.

### Solvency\-\--No Adoption\-\--2NC

#### SMRs fail\-\--

#### 1. [EMPIRICS]{.underline}.

**Makhajani '21** \[Arjun, M.V. Ramana; July 21; Arjun Makhaljani is a
Fellow of American Physical society with a PhD in diffusion from UC
Berkeley, M.V. Ramana is a Global Affairs professor at the University of
British Columbia, "Can small modular reactors help mitigate climate
change,"
https://thebulletin.org/premium/2021-07/can-small-modular-reactors-help-mitigate-climate-change/\]

The [**[s]{.mark}**mall **[m]{.mark}**odular **[r]{.mark}**eactor [track
record]{.mark}]{.underline} so far [[points to]{.underline}]{.mark} the
same kind of **[[dismal economic failure]{.underline}]{.mark}** as for
their larger cousins.

The [[US E]{.mark}nergy [D]{.mark}epartment [has been pursuing
s]{.mark}mall [m]{.mark}odular [r]{.mark}eactor[s]{.mark}
[since]{.mark}]{.underline} the last century. In
**[[2000]{.underline}]{.mark}**, the US Congress provided funding "to
undertake a study to determine the feasibility of and issues associated
with the deployment of... small reactors" (Department of Energy 2001,
1; *Congressional Record* 2001). The Energy Department's Office of
Nuclear Energy 2001 report reviewed nearly ten designs and concluded
that "the most technically mature small modular reactor (SMR) designs
and concepts have the potential to be economical and could be made
available for deployment before the end of the decade, provided that
certain technical and licensing issues are addressed" (Department of
Energy 2001, iii).

The US Nuclear Regulatory Commission has been similarly optimistic. In
October 2008, it projected that the certification review for the NuScale
design would be completed by 2015. It estimated that it would also
complete reviews for other designs, including the Pebble Bed Modular
Reactor and the Hyperion reactor (currently Gen4 Energy) in the same
time frame (Baker 2008). None of that happened.

These [[rosy predictions **failed to materialize** despite]{.mark}
substantial [government support]{.mark}]{.underline}. In the early
2010s, the [[Energy Department supported two]{.mark} small modular
reactor [designs]{.mark}]{.underline}: mPower by Babcock & Wilcox and
NuScale. [[The first]{.mark} of these [was]{.mark} a **[complete
failure]{.mark};**]{.underline} after [years of trying to get investors
and funding]{.underline}, the mPower [program was
terminated]{.underline} (Downey 2015; Adams 2017).

NuScale, [[another recipient]{.mark} of government
largesse]{.underline}, has fared a little better. But the [earliest
official projected [deployment date is]{.mark}]{.underline} now [2029 or
2030]{.underline}---more than a decade later than the Energy
Department's estimate. Even [that date is [**highly uncertain**
because]{.mark}]{.underline} the Nuclear Regulatory Commission's
Advisory [Committee on Reactor Safeguards]{.underline} has [identified
**[serious safety concerns]{.mark}** that will [have to be
addressed]{.mark}]{.underline} before any utility applies for permission
to construct a small modular reactor (Advisory Committee on Reactor
Safeguards 2020b; 2020a; NRC 2020). Significantly, a central concern
involves the steam generator, which, as noted above, is inside the
reactor vessel and a potential source of operational and economic
problems.

#### 2. [REGS]{.underline}.

**Makhajani '21** \[Arjun, M.V. Ramana; July 21; Arjun Makhaljani is a
Fellow of American Physical society with a PhD in diffusion from UC
Berkeley, M.V. Ramana is a Global Affairs professor at the University of
British Columbia, "Can small modular reactors help mitigate climate
change,"
https://thebulletin.org/premium/2021-07/can-small-modular-reactors-help-mitigate-climate-change/\]

[[New designs]{.mark} will [mean]{.mark}]{.underline} that the process
of [getting [safety approvals]{.mark}]{.underline} []{.mark}will likely
[[**take longer** and be **more expensive**]{.underline}]{.mark}. In
many cases, [[even setting up]{.mark} sound [certification]{.mark}
process [will take **years**]{.mark},]{.underline} since the [safety and
accident modes differ with **each basic design**]{.underline}. For
instance, one risk with high-temperature gas-graphite reactors is fires
rather than meltdowns.

[To give a sense of financial scale]{.underline}, the development of the
[[NuScale]{.mark} [s]{.mark}]{.underline}mall
[[m]{.underline}]{.mark}odular [[r]{.underline}]{.mark}eactor, which is
the familiar light water design, is [[expected to cost]{.mark} roughly
\$[**1.5 billion** for]{.mark}]{.underline} development and
[[certification]{.underline}]{.mark} (Hopkins 2020; Chaffee 2020).
[Completely [new]{.mark} non-light water reactor
[designs]{.mark}]{.underline} envisioned by some small modular reactor
developers [[will]{.mark} very likely [**cost even more** and
take]{.mark} **even [longer]{.mark}** [to translate]{.mark}]{.underline}
from the conceptual stage to designs licensed for construction.

#### 3. [WASTE]{.underline}.

**Makhajani '21** \[Arjun, M.V. Ramana; July 21; Arjun Makhaljani is a
Fellow of American Physical society with a PhD in diffusion from UC
Berkeley, M.V. Ramana is a Global Affairs professor at the University of
British Columbia, "Can small modular reactors help mitigate climate
change,"
https://thebulletin.org/premium/2021-07/can-small-modular-reactors-help-mitigate-climate-change/\]

Finally, [[s]{.underline}]{.mark}mall [[m]{.underline}]{.mark}odular
[[r]{.underline}]{.mark}eactor[[s]{.underline}]{.mark}, like their
larger counterparts, will [also [produce
**radioactive**]{.mark}]{.underline} nuclear [[waste]{.mark}s; [many
will produce more]{.mark}]{.underline} per unit of [power generated [for
**technical** and **economic** reasons]{.mark}]{.underline} (Glaser,
Hopkins, and Ramana 2013; Brown, Worrall, and Todosow 2017; Krall 2020).
The [US government]{.underline} is already [[pay]{.mark}ing [**billions
of dollars** in fines]{.mark}]{.underline} for [not
fulfilling]{.underline} its [contractual obligations]{.underline} to
take possession of spent fuel from existing reactors. The legislative
plan in the 1982 Nuclear Waste Policy Act was for a deep geologic
disposal repository to open in 1998. All of the plans in that act have
come to naught after nearly four decades of effort.

[[Waste is]{.underline}]{.mark} also [[a problem
for]{.underline}]{.mark} non-light water [[reactor
designs]{.mark}.]{.underline} Many sodium-cooled reactors, for example,
use metallic sodium fuel that cannot be disposed of in geological
repositories without extensive processing (Krall and MacFarlane 2018).
Waste from molten salt reactors will be highly radioactive due to
fission products that were not vented in the off-gas and will include
halides like uranium tetrafluoride. If such waste forms are buried as
without processing, and  they interact with water, they would produce
corrosive hydrofluoric acid (Krall and MacFarlane 2018). Therefore,
[[they would need to be processed **before
disposal**]{.underline}]{.mark} using [processes [that will be
**costly** and **complex**]{.mark}]{.underline}; moreover, such
[[processing has **never been carried out** at
scale.]{.underline}]{.mark}

#### 4. [DEMAND]{.underline}.

**Makhajani '21** \[Arjun, M.V. Ramana; July 21; Arjun Makhaljani is a
Fellow of American Physical society with a PhD in diffusion from UC
Berkeley, M.V. Ramana is a Global Affairs professor at the University of
British Columbia, "Can small modular reactors help mitigate climate
change,"
https://thebulletin.org/premium/2021-07/can-small-modular-reactors-help-mitigate-climate-change/\]

One **[economic problem]{.underline}** with [[s]{.mark}mall
[m]{.mark}odular [r]{.mark}eactors]{.underline} arises from the very
concept. This was [made clear]{.underline} by the developers of the
STAR-H2 design who pointed out [that "the [business risk]{.mark} [would
be transferred]{.mark}]{.underline} predominately [[to the
**supplier**]{.underline}]{.mark} who [must initially emplace a large
factory]{.underline} for economy of mass production fabrication" and
''\[a\] [[supplier would have to foresee]{.mark} a [sufficient
market]{.mark}]{.underline} to invest in factories large enough [to
achieve economy of mass production]{.underline} from production runs of
many hundreds of turnkey plants''  (IAEA 2007, 688). But [[electricity
demand **projections are difficult**]{.mark}; demand has
been]{.underline} consistently [overestimated since the 1970s. [That is
why]{.mark}]{.underline} close to **[[100
reactors]{.underline}]{.mark}** that were [[planned]{.underline}]{.mark}
or under construction [[were **cancelled**]{.underline}]{.mark} in the
first wave of nuclear construction.

One noteworthy problem with small modular reactors is related to the
supply chain. A mistake for a large reactor affects the utility that
made the error. Significant errors in grid-level projections needed to
generate a large enough order book and sustain the supply chain, would
mean skyrocketing costs for all customers or losses for suppliers---or
both.

[[Electricity from s]{.underline}]{.mark}mall
[[m]{.underline}]{.mark}odular
[[r]{.underline}]{.mark}eactor[[s]{.underline}]{.mark} [[is **too
expensive**]{.underline}]{.mark} even [[in niche markets]{.mark} such

#### 1 -- [ECONOMIES OF SCALE]{.underline} -- SMR's cost [more]{.underline} and [sacrifice safety requirements]{.underline} to be [cheaper]{.underline}. 

Edwin **Lyman'24** - internationally recognized expert on nuclear
proliferation and nuclear power safety and security. He is a member of
the Institute of Nuclear Materials Management and has testified numerous
times before Congress and the Nuclear Regulatory Commission. (\"Five
Things the "Nuclear Bros" Don't Want You to Know About Small Modular
Reactors\", Union of Concerned Scientists,
https://blog.ucs.org/edwin-lyman/five-things-the-nuclear-bros-dont-want-you-to-know-about-small-modular-reactors/,
4-30-2024, Accessed 7-1-2025)//Ilake-EO

Even casual followers of energy and climate issues have probably heard
about [the [**alleged wonders** of]{.mark} small modular nuclear
reactors]{.underline} [([SMRs]{.mark}).]{.underline} This is due in no
small part to the "nuclear bros": an active and seemingly tireless group
of nuclear power advocates who dominate social media discussions on
energy by promoting SMRs and other "advanced" nuclear technologies as
the only real solution for the climate crisis. But as I showed in my
2013 and 2021 reports, the hype surrounding SMRs [is]{.underline} way
**[[overblown]{.underline}]{.mark}**, and my conclusions remain valid
today.

Unfortunately, [much of this SMR happy talk is [rooted in
**misinformation**]{.mark}]{.underline}, which always brings me back to
the same question: If the nuclear bros have such a great SMR story to
tell, why do they have to exaggerate so much?

What are SMRs?

SMRs are nuclear reactors that are "small" (defined as 300 megawatts of
electrical power or less), can be largely assembled in a centralized
facility, and would be installed in a modular fashion at power
generation sites. Some proposed SMRs are so tiny (20 megawatts or less)
that they are called "micro" reactors. SMRs are distinct from today's
conventional nuclear plants, which are typically around 1,000 megawatts
and were largely custom-built. Some SMR designs, such as NuScale, are
modified versions of operating water-cooled reactors, while others are
radically different designs that use coolants other than water, such as
liquid sodium, helium gas, or even molten salts.

To date, however, [[theoretical interest in SMRs]{.mark} has [not
translated into]{.mark}]{.underline} many [[actual
reactor]{.underline}]{.mark} orders. [The only SMR]{.underline}
currently under construction [is in China]{.underline}. And [in the
United States, only one company]{.underline}---TerraPower, founded by
Microsoft's Bill Gates---[has applied to]{.underline} the Nuclear
Regulatory Commission (NRC) for a permit to [build a power
reactor]{.underline} (but at 345 megawatts, [it technically
isn't]{.underline} even [an SMR).]{.underline}

The nuclear industry has pinned its hopes on SMRs primarily because some
recent large reactor projects, including Vogtle units 3 and 4 in the
state of Georgia, have taken far longer to build and cost far more than
originally projected. The failure of these projects to come in on time
and under budget undermines arguments that modern nuclear power plants
can overcome the problems that have plagued the nuclear industry in the
past.

Developers in the industry and the US Department of Energy say that SMRs
can be less costly and quicker to build than large reactors and that
their modular nature makes it easier to balance power supply and demand.
They also argue that reactors in a variety of sizes would be useful for
a range of applications beyond grid-scale electrical power, including
providing process heat to industrial plants and power to data centers,
cryptocurrency mining operations, petrochemical production, and even
electrical vehicle charging stations.

[Here are five facts about SMRs]{.underline} that [the nuclear industry
and the "**nuclear bros**]{.underline}" who push its message [don't
want]{.underline} you, [the public, to know.]{.underline}

1\. [[SMRs are **not**]{.mark} **more [economical]{.mark}** than large
reactors]{.underline}.

[[In theory]{.underline}]{.mark}, [small reactors [should have
lower]{.mark} capital [costs]{.mark}]{.underline} and construction times
than large reactors of similar design [so that utilities]{.underline}
(or other users) [can get financing]{.underline} more [cheaply and
deploy them]{.underline} more [flexibly]{.underline}. But [that doesn't
mean small reactors will be more]{.underline} [economical]{.underline}
than large ones. In fact, the [**[opposite]{.mark}** usually will be
[true]{.mark}]{.underline}. [What matters more [when
comparing]{.mark}]{.underline} the [[economics of different power
sources]{.mark} is the cost to produce]{.underline} a [kilowatt-hour of
electricity, [and]{.mark}]{.underline} [that depends on]{.underline} the
capital cost per kilowatt of generating capacity, as well as the costs
of operations, maintenance, fuel, and [other factors]{.underline}.

[According to]{.underline} the **[[economies of
scale]{.underline}]{.mark}** principle, [[smaller reactors]{.mark}
will]{.underline} in general [[produce **more expensive
electricity**]{.mark} than larger ones]{.underline}. For example, [the
**now-cancelled** project]{.underline} by NuScale to [build a
460-megawatt, 6-unit [SMR]{.mark}]{.underline} in Idaho [was estimated
to [cost]{.mark} over]{.underline} [\$20,000 per kilowatt]{.underline},
which is [**[greater than]{.mark} the actual [cost]{.mark}** [of]{.mark}
the Vogtle [large reactor project]{.mark}]{.underline} of over \$15,000
per kilowatt. This cost penalty can be offset only by radical changes in
the way reactors are designed, built, and operated.

For example, [[SMR developers claim they]{.mark}]{.underline} can [slash
capital cost]{.underline} per kilowatt [by [achiev]{.mark}ing
[efficiency]{.mark} through]{.underline} the [mass
production]{.underline} of identical units in factories. [However,
[**studies** find]{.mark}]{.underline} that such [[cost
reductions]{.mark}]{.underline} typically [would **[not exceed]{.mark}
about [30%]{.mark}.**]{.underline} In addition, [[dozens of
units]{.mark} would [have to be]{.mark} [produced before
manufacturers]{.mark}]{.underline} could [[learn how to
make]{.underline}]{.mark} their [[processes]{.underline}]{.mark} more
[[efficient]{.mark} and]{.underline} [achieve]{.underline} those capital
[cost reductions, meaning]{.underline} that the [[first
reactors]{.underline}]{.mark} of a given design [will be [unavoidably
expensive]{.mark}]{.underline} and will [[require]{.underline} [large
**government**]{.underline}]{.mark} [**or ratepayer [subsidies]{.mark}**
to get built]{.underline}. Getting past this obstacle has proven to be
one of the main impediments to SMR deployment.

[Another way]{.underline} that [[SMR developers]{.mark} try to
[reduce]{.mark}]{.underline} capital [[cost]{.mark} is [by]{.mark}
reducing]{.underline} [or **[eliminating]{.mark}**]{.underline} many of
the [**[safety features]{.mark}** required for operating reactors [that
provide]{.mark}]{.underline} multiple layers of
[[protection]{.underline}]{.mark}, [such as]{.underline} a robust,
[reinforced concrete containment structure]{.underline}, motor-driven
[emergency pumps]{.underline}, and rigorous quality assurance standards
for backup safety equipment such as power supplies. But these changes so
far haven't had much of an impact on the overall cost---just look at
NuScale.

### Solvency\-\--No Adoption\-\--No Spillover\-\--2NC

#### [No global spillover]{.underline}. SMR's are [costly]{.underline}, provide [small amounts of energy]{.underline}, and have been tested everywhere but [failed]{.underline}. 

Jim **Green '24**\-\--Dr. Jim Green is the national nuclear campaigner
with Friends of the Earth Australia, a member of the Nuclear Consulting
Group, and is former editor of the World Information Service on Energy's
Nuclear Monitor newsletter. (\"Small modular nuclear reactors: a history
of failure\", Climate and Capital Media,
https://www.climateandcapitalmedia.com/small-modular-nuclear-reactors-a-history-of-failure/,
1-17-2024). Accessed 7-6-2025 by NT-RM.

SMRs so far? Shut down

[The **history of small reactors** is a **history of failure**. The U.S.
Army built and operated eight small reactors beginning in the 1950s, but
they proved **unreliable** and **expensive** and the program was **shut
down** in 1977. In addition, 17 small civilian reactors were built in
the US in the 1950s and 1960s, but all have since shut
down.]{.underline}

[Twenty-six small]{.underline} Magnox [reactors were built in the
**U.K.** but all]{.underline} have [**shut down** and **no more will be
built**]{.underline}. The only operating Magnox is a mini-Magnox in
North Korea: the design was made public at an Atoms for Peace conference
and North Korea uses its 5 MW Magnox to produce plutonium for nuclear
weapons.

[India operates 14 small pressurized heavy water reactors, each with a
capacity of about 200 MW]{.underline}. Professor M.V. Ramana noted in
his 2012 book, "The Power of Promise: Examining Nuclear Energy in
India," that [despite a standardized approach to designing, constructing
and operating these reactors, many **suffered cost overruns** and
**lengthy delays**. There are **no plans to build more** of these small
reactors **in India**.]{.underline}

[Nuclear power's share of global electricity generation has **nearly
halved** from **17.5%** in 1996 to **9.2%** now]{.underline}

[Elsewhere, the history of small reactors is just as **underwhelming**.
This includes three small reactors in Canada (all shut down), six in
France (all shut down) and four in Japan (all shut down).]{.underline}

Ramana concludes his history of small reactors with this downbeat
assessment: "Without exception, [**small reactors cost too** much for
the **little electricity they produce**, the result of both their **low
output** and their **poor performance."**]{.underline}

[Just two SMR plants are said to be operating]{.underline} -- [neither
meeting the "modular" definition of serial factory production of reactor
components]{.underline}. [These so-called SMRs exhibit familiar problems
of **massive cost blowouts** and **multi-year delays.**]{.underline}

[The **construction cost** of Russia's floating twin-reactor plant
**increased six-fold** and]{.underline} the OECD's Nuclear Energy Agency
estimates that the [electricity]{.underline} it produces [costs
**\$200**]{.underline} per megawatt-hour (**[MWh]{.underline}**). The
reactor is used to power fossil fuel mining operations in the Arctic.

[The other operating SMR]{.underline} -- loosely defined -- [is China's
demonstration twin-reactor]{.underline} high-temperature gas-cooled
reactor (HTGR). The World Nuclear Association states that [the cost of
the demonstration HTGR was **\$6,000 per kilowatt**, **three times
higher** than early cost estimates and **two to three times higher**
than the cost of China's larger Hualong reactors per
kilowatt.]{.underline}

NucNet reported in 2020 that China dropped plans to manufacture 20 HTGRs
after levelised [cost estimates **rose to levels higher than
conventional large reactors**]{.underline}. Likewise, the World Nuclear
Association states that plans for 18 additional HTGRs at the same site
as the demonstration HTGR have been "dropped". [China's demonstration
HTGR demonstrates yet again that the **economics of small reactors
doesn't stack up**.]{.underline}

[Three SMRs are under construction -- again with the qualification that
there's **nothing 'modular'** about these projects.]{.underline}

[Argentina's]{.underline} CAREM [reactor has been a
**disaster**]{.underline}. [Construction began in 2014 and the National
Atomic Energy Commission now hopes to complete the reactor in
2027]{.underline} ‒ [nearly **50 years** after the project was
conceived. The cost estimate]{.underline} in 2021 [was **\$750 million
for a reactor** with a capacity of just **32 MW**.]{.underline} [That's
a **huge expense for a reactor** with the capacity of a handful of large
wind turbines.]{.underline}

In 2021, [China began construction of a]{.underline} 125 MW [pressurized
water reactor]{.underline}. According to China National Nuclear
Corporation, [construction costs per kilowatt will be **twice the cost
of large reactors**, and levelised costs will be **50% higher** than
large reactors.]{.underline}

"[Without exception, **small reactors cost too much** for the **little
electricity they produce**]{.underline}"

Also in 2021, construction of the 300 MW demonstration lead-cooled BREST
fast neutron reactor began in Russia. The [cost estimate has more than
doubled]{.underline} to 100 billion rubles (US\$1.1 billion) and [no
doubt it will **continue to climb.**]{.underline}

### Solvency\-\--No Adoption\-\--Cost\-\--2NC

#### Empirics prove that SMR's are [too costly to deploy]{.underline} and face [significant construction delays]{.underline}. 

David **Schlissel and** Dennis **Wamsted '24**\-\--David Schlissel is
the former director of resource planning analysis for IEEFA and
co-founder of the organization, a regulatory attorney and consultant.
Dennis Wamsted is the energy analyst at the Institute for Energy

5-29-2024). Accessed 7-6-2025 by NT-RM.

[The rhetoric from small modular reactor (SMR) advocates is loud and
persistent: This time will be different because the cost overruns and
schedule delays]{.underline} that have plagued large reactor
construction projects [will not be repeated]{.underline} with the new
designs[. But **the few SMRs that have been built** (or have been
started) paint a different picture---one that looks startingly **similar
to the past**.]{.underline} [**Significant construction delays** are
still the norm and **costs have continued to climb.**]{.underline}

[IEEFA has taken a close look at the **data available from the four
SMRs** currently in operation]{.underline} or under construction, [as
well as new information about projected costs from some of the leading
SMR developers in the U.S. The results]{.underline} of the analysis
[show **little has changed** from our previous work. SMRs still are

#### SMR's are [costly]{.underline} and other renewable sources are [better for the environment.]{.underline} 

Paul **Hockenos '24**\-\--Paul Hockenos is a Berlin-based journalist and
author of Berlin Calling. (\"The illusion of small nuclear reactors
undermines climate protection\", *Energy Transition*,
https://energytransition.org/2024/02/the-illusion-of-small-nuclear-reactors-undermines-climate-protection/,
2-22-2024). Accessed 7-1-2025 by NT-RM.

But [despite the]{.underline} prodigious [chatter about a comeback of
nuclear, the endeavor to blanket the Earth with SMRs is a chimera that
**contradicts** the same established body of fact that the larger models
do]{.underline}, as reported in Undark, a media portal out of MIT. [That
this rollout won't happen]{.underline} -- [or if it eventually does,
will **unfold too late** to curb the climate crisis]{.underline} --
[might look like a **loss** to none other than the nuclear industry and
investors]{.underline} like Gates.

But its fallout is much broader: [nuclear's ostensible revival, whether
it pans out or not, **diverts critical resources** from the urgent task
at hand, namely the ever more important **buildout of clean
techn**ologies on hand and affordable]{.underline}.

"[Much of the battle to meet the **challenge of climate
change**]{.underline}," argued Mark Cooper of Vermont Law School in a
report subtitled, "Why Nuclear Subsidies are an Unnecessary Threat to
the Transformation," "[will **be over before** even **one of these
reactors is online**."]{.underline}

The most probable scenario is that these ideas never get off the ground.
Currently, there are just two facilities that roughly resemble the
envisioned SMR type, a pilot in China and Russia's diminutive Akademik
Lomonosov, also the world's first floating nuclear power plant (a dark
irony, its primary purpose is to power fossil fuel mining operations in
the Arctic.) Because the Akademik Lomonosov is Russian, it circumvented
design certification as required in North America and Europe, and is now
because of tension over the Ukraine war beyond the purview of Western
experts.

More small reactors are under construction in China and Russia, as well
as one in Argentina, but all of them are proving more expensive pro
kilowatt than traditional reactors. (There is a dedicated chapter on
SMRs in the most recent WNISR 2023.)

[In the U.S., the only developer with a design approved by the Nuclear
Regulatory Commission is NuScale, which **hasn't yet laid a single
brick** in Idaho, where it plans to build. Its big win thus far is \$4
billion in federal tax subsidies that include a \$1.4 billion U.S.
Department of Energy contribution and the IRA's \$30/MWh
credit]{.underline}, according to the US-based Institute for Energy
Economics and Financial Analysis (IEEFA).

This year, [NuScale announced that a megawatt of electricity **will not
cost \$58/MWh**]{.underline}, as originally pledged, [but **rather
\$89/MWh**, because of construction costs now estimated at **\$9.3
billion rather than \$6.3 billion**]{.underline}. [BloombergNEF's
calculates that average global cost of **utility-scale solar** and
**onshore wind** at **\$45** and **\$46** per megawatt-hour,
respectively]{.underline}. Were the NuScale reactor to omit the
government subsidies, its price tag would be that much higher.

As expected, no NuScale SMR will be built in Idaho-- since it announced
that it will not begin construction until all of its expected generation
capacity is subscribed. Before the price hike, around 50 utilities and
other buyers have signed up for less than a quarter of capacity. In
November 2023, NuScale finally cancelled its contract with a US utility
for building its first SMR in Idaho.

Gates's TerraPower isn't even as far as NuScale, although it too is
cashing in on subsidies: the U.S. Department of Energy's Advanced
Reactor Demonstration Program has pledged up to \$2 billion in matching
project costs for the construction of a Natrium-cooled demonstration
plant (modern nuclear power plants use water as a coolant) in Wyoming.
But the Gates project recently took a hit: it faces delays of at least
two years because its only source of fuel, namely uranium, was Russia.

[There's no reason that SMRs, like large reactors, will not be subject
to **egregious delays** and **cost overruns**, valid **safety concerns**
and the conundrum of **radioactive waste disposal**.]{.underline}

Dr. Mark Z. **Jacobson 24**, Professor of Civil and Environmental

#### The [best-case scenario]{.underline} is a decade.

What are SMRs?

SMRs are nuclear reactors that are "small" (defined as 300 megawatts of
electrical power or less), can be largely assembled in a centralized
facility, and would be installed in a modular fashion at power
generation sites. Some proposed SMRs are so tiny (20 megawatts or less)
that they are called "micro" reactors. SMRs are distinct from today's
conventional nuclear plants, which are typically around 1,000 megawatts
and were largely custom-built. Some SMR designs, such as NuScale, are
modified versions of operating water-cooled reactors, while others are
radically different designs that use coolants other than water, such as
liquid sodium, helium gas, or even molten salts.

[To date]{.underline}, however, [[theoretical interest in SMRs **has not
translated** into]{.mark} many **[actual reactor
orders]{.mark}**]{.underline}[. [The only SMR currently under
construction is in China]{.underline}]{.mark}[. And in the United
States, only one company---TerraPower]{.underline}, founded by
Microsoft's Bill Gates---[has applied to the Nuclear Regulatory
Commission (NRC) for a permit to build a power reactor]{.underline}
([but]{.underline} at 345 megawatts, [it technically isn't even an
SMR]{.underline}).

in 2019. (Plutonium disposition means destroying weapons-usable

Dr. M.V. **Ramana 24**, PhD in Physics from Boston University, Professor

### Solvency\-\--SMRs Fake\-\--AT: New tech\-\--2NC

#### "Advanced" reactors are all [hype]{.underline}\-\--they're [universally not ready]{.underline} for large-scale power generation.

Fanny **Böse et al. 24**, holds an M.S. in Industrial Engineering from
Technical University Berlin, works at the Federal Office for the Safety
of Nuclear Waste Management (BASE) in Germany, "Questioning nuclear
scale-up propositions: Availability and economic prospects of light
water, small modular and advanced reactor technologies", Energy Research
& Social Science, Volume 110, April 2024, 103448

This paper addresses the pressing need for rapid decarbonization of
energy systems by examining the role of nuclear power in future energy
scenarios collected in the IPCC database. Despite their explorative
character, energy scenarios can influence decision-makers or might be
used to promote certain technology developments. They are increasingly
subject to the integration into real-world settings and thus the need
for feasibility assessments has emerged in literature. [The case of
nuclear power is of particular interest, for which proponents project
steep capacity increases]{.underline}, [whereas]{.underline}
**[literature]{.underline}** [shows a **technology in
decline**]{.underline}. The paper investigates this discrepancy and
questions the nuclear scale-up in energy scenarios by investigating
proposed technology scale-up thresholds for low carbon scenarios. By
doing so, it moves beyond the often-applied aggregate view of nuclear
power and explores the techno-economic feasibility from a
reactor-technology-specific lens. [Three reactor technologies are
chosen]{.underline}: currently dominant [high-capacity light water
reactors]{.underline}, [small modular reactors]{.underline}, [and
non-light water-cooled reactors]{.underline} ([so-called **"new" or
"advanced"** reactor concepts]{.underline}). [Results indicate that
**[none of the]{.mark} currently [proposed reactor
technologies]{.mark}**]{.underline} [[are]{.underline} **[fit
for]{.underline}**]{.mark} **[necessary rapid
[decarbonization]{.mark}]{.underline}** [[due to **availability
constraints and economic challenges**]{.underline}]{.mark}. We further
suggest the adaptation of proposed thresholds to absolute metrics based
on energy generation and towards the inclusion of a sub-technology
specific perspective. Overall, [the need for further
**[scrutiny]{.mark}**]{.underline} [of energy scenarios]{.underline},
particularly **[concerning nuclear power]{.underline}**, [[is
emphasized]{.mark} in order [to prevent
**instrumentalization**]{.mark}]{.underline} [[by]{.underline}]{.mark}
**[political]{.underline}** [and]{.underline} **[[industry
actors.]{.underline}]{.mark}**

The urgency of climate change requires a rapid decarbonization of energy
systems until 2050 \[1\]. In this context, energy scenarios are a useful
tool to explore variations of future energy systems and show possible
pathways towards the achievement thereof \[2\]. However, despite their
explorative character, energy scenarios can influence decision-makers or
might be used to promote certain technology developments \[3,4\].
Consequently, energy scenarios are increasingly subject to attempts to
be integrated into real-world settings. The assessment of the
feasibility of such implementations has thus emerged in literature
\[\[5\], \[6\], \[7\]\].

The role of nuclear power in future energy systems is especially
controversial. While some research suggests that future energy systems
can function with renewable energies only, such as wind and solar
\[\[8\], \[9\], \[10\]\], others include nuclear power as an option
\[\[11\], \[12\], \[13\]\] or even as a required complement to
renewables \[14,15\]. **[[Experience]{.underline}]{.mark}** however
[[shows nuclear]{.underline}]{.mark} power [**[to be a technology in
decline]{.underline}**, [and a nuclear industry that will **likely not
be able to live up to expectations**]{.underline}
[of]{.underline}]{.mark} [a **[rapid capacity
scale-up]{.mark}**]{.underline} \[16,17\].

Nonetheless, [proponents envision a substantial nuclear scale-up by
establishing **["novel" technologies]{.mark}**]{.underline},
[[including]{.mark} concepts for so-called ["small modular

#### All the 'new' advanced reactor designs have already been [tried]{.underline}\-\--and they [failed]{.underline}.

[[The]{.underline} **[false promise of advanced
reactors]{.underline}**]{.mark}

[When]{.underline} such [problems are highlighted, nuclear [proponents

the]{.mark}]{.underline} [**[past]{.underline}**]{.mark} [and are

[reactors, [or "**generation-IV**"]{.mark} reactors]{.underline}, or

issues.]{.underline}

The term "advanced" is arguably the most sweeping of these. If one goes
to their historical origins, even the large reactor designs being
offered by US nuclear reactor companies can be termed advanced. That's
because these reactor designs all benefitted from the US Department of
Energy's Advanced Light Water Reactor Program initiated in the 1980s.
That program aimed to "restore nuclear power as a viable option" in the
1990s, by producing "improved and simplified" reactor designs that were
to be "economically attractive, and constructable on a predictable and
reasonable schedule," an official from the DOE told the 1990 American
Power Conference. This program underwrote the AP1000 reactors that
proved disastrous to the consumers of Georgia and South Carolina (see
chapter 3).

[These days nuclear advocates shift the definition and use terms like
"advanced" to refer to reactor designs not cooled by water. That cooling
role is played by gases like helium,]{.underline} [or molten metals like
sodium,]{.underline} [or by molten chemical compounds called
salts]{.underline} (of which common salt---sodium chloride---is an
example). [Many of these reactor designs also fit into the category of
small modular reactors]{.underline} because they would produce less than
300 megawatts of electricity.

[of]{.underline}]{.mark} [nuclear [designers have **explored**]{.mark}
**just about [all these types of
reactors]{.mark}**]{.underline}[---[and]{.underline} **[abandoned
them]{.underline}** [because of **technical or operational
problems**]{.underline}]{.mark}. It is impossible to go through each
reactor design, but I will explain some of the problems that afflict
three generic categories of reactors.

### Warming\-\--No Solvency\-\--1NC

#### SMR's will have [zero impact]{.underline} on solving climate [in the next decade.]{.underline}

Arjun **Makhijani and** M.V. **Ramana '21**\-\--Arjun Makhijani is the
president of the IEER and has a Ph.D in engineering. M.V. Ramana is the

SMRs and the climate crisis

[The climate problem is **urgent**. The IPCC and other international
bodies have warned that to stop irreversible damage from climate change,
we need to **reduce emissions drastically** within the **next
decade**]{.underline}. [The SMR contribution]{.underline} in the next
decade [will be essentially **zero**]{.underline}. [The prospects for
SMRs]{.underline} beyond that [are]{.underline} also
**[bleak]{.underline}**, [given that entire **supply chains would need
to be established** after the first ones have been built, tested and
proven in the field]{.underline}.

[The D]{.underline}epartment [o]{.underline}f [E]{.underline}nergy [has
been pursuing SMRs since the **last century**. In 2001, the DOE's Office
of Nuclear Energy projected that there were nearly 10 SMR designs that
"have the potential to be economical]{.underline} and could be made
available for deployment before the end of the decade, provided that
certain technical and licensing issues are addressed."

[Nearly **two decades after**]{.underline} that rosy notion, [the
earliest official projected deployment date is only **2029** to
**2030**]{.underline} for the leading design, NuScale. [Even that date
is **highly uncertain**, because the **N**uclear **R**egulatory
**C**ommission's Advisory Committee on Reactor Safeguards has identified
**serious safety concerns** that will have to be addressed before any
utility applies for permission to construct an SMR]{.underline}.
Significantly, [a central concern involves the **steam generator**,
which]{.underline}, as noted above, [is inside the reactor vessel and a
potential source of **reliability** and **economic
problems.**]{.underline}

[SMRs]{.underline} also [divert **valuable public money**]{.underline}.
For example, [the federal government has contributed]{.underline} at
least [**\$314 million** to the development of the NuScale SMR design
and has reportedly agreed to spend up to **\$350 million**]{.underline}
in new matching funds. [Babcock & Wilcox received over **\$100 million**
from the DOE for its mPower design but **abandoned the project** in 2017
because there were **no customers.**]{.underline}

1\. [[Nuclear]{.mark} energy [delivers **too little to
