### Leadership\-\--Alt Causes\-\--1NC

#### Alt causes to nuclear leadership\-\--Trump's abandoning allies, that probably doesn't help our position even if we have marginally better reactors.

#### AND\-\--[licensing]{.underline} prevents effective leadership.

Dr. Ariel **Cohen 24**, Ph.D., is a Senior Fellow at the Atlantic
Council and the Founding Principal of International Market Analysis, a
Washington, D.C.-based global risk advisory boutique. He is also
Managing Director of the Energy, Growth, and Security Program (EGS) and
a Senior Fellow with the International Tax and Investment Center (ITIC),
"The U.S. Is Losing the Nuclear Energy Race to Russia and China",
https://www.forbes.com/sites/arielcohen/2024/01/25/the-us-is-losing-the-nuclear-energy-race-to-russia-and-china/

[While the U.S. has coasted on its laurels]{.underline}, with nuclear
energy production not changing much in over 30 years,
[**[Russia]{.underline}** [continues its]{.underline}]{.mark} [gradual
[climb upward]{.mark}]{.underline} and exports many reactors,
[while]{.underline} [**[China]{.underline}** [is investing
heavily]{.underline}]{.mark} [in civilian nuclear]{.underline} tech and
boosting its atomic power generation at home. Beijing plans to build 24
new nuclear power plants by 2030, bringing the total up to 60,
overtaking the U.S. with its old reactor fleet. For comparison, the U.S.
has 93 operational nuclear power plants in total, and in the same period
as China's building spree, the U.S. added 2 with none under development
now.

[[This]{.mark} decline [is **by choice**]{.mark}]{.underline}. The U.S.
certainly used to have the technical and financial resources to compete
with Russia and China in these fields, but the political will to pursue
nuclear power (despite its overwhelmingly green characteristics (zero
CO2 emissions, and its exemplary safety record in North America) has
been lacking. The notorious Three Mile Island accident in 1979, killed,
injured, irradiated, or harmed zero people. Meanwhile, ballooning costs
and [[the]{.underline} **[nightmare that is America's
byzantine]{.underline}**]{.mark} **[environmental [review and permitting
processes]{.mark}]{.underline}** [[resulted in the last]{.mark} nuclear
power [plant arriving **7 years late**]{.mark}]{.underline}
[[and]{.underline} **[17 billion]{.underline}**]{.mark} **[dollars [over
budget]{.mark}]{.underline}**[. [It's **little surprise** the U.S. is
falling behind]{.underline}]{.mark} [in many nuclear areas.]{.underline}
"A generation of Americans were scared by Three Mile Island, and the
nuclear industry became a political and environmental target", said Jack
Edlow of Edlow International, a company that specializes in the global
transportation of nuclear materials and has been in the nuclear industry
for 55 years.

The average age of a nuclear reactor in the U.S. is 40 years old,
meaning Russia and China have been able to take advantage of (often
American) research into better reactors. The emerging technology of
Small Modular Reactors (SMRs) and advanced generation four reactors are
being embraced far quicker in China and Russia than in the U.S.

SMRs are a popular option to advance clean and safe production, as they
address multiple concerns about nuclear power. The ease of assembly and
transport of SMRs changes the construction of a reactor from a
multi-billion dollar, multi-year process into a shorter and cheaper
endeavor with timelier returns. SMRs also lower environmental risks due
to their anticipated reduced rate of accidents and lack of meltdown
potential due to innovative technology. Additionally, environmental and
aesthetic concerns are addressed by the ability of SMRs to be placed in
remote areas, or underground. Despite the promise of SMRs and platitudes
from the DOE on the subject, investments in SMRs lag far behind what is
needed.

Even with an updated fleet of reactors, the United States would still be
falling behind Russia in its supply of high-assay, low-enriched uranium
(HALEU), which is enriched up to 20% compared to the average 5% of
uranium used in current reactors to allow more efficient units to
function. The commercial supply of HALEU is currently controlled by
TENEX, a division of the state-owned Russian nuclear energy company
Rosatom. To bolster America's domestic production, the Biden
administration has put out an RFP for HALEU enrichment. The process of
building a domestic supply will be difficult to jumpstart with only one
U.S. company, Centrus Energy, being licensed to refine HALEU and one
European company, Urenco, planning to start production in New Mexico.
The RFP will allow many companies other than CENTRUS to bid for the new
plant operation but operation is not likely to occur until the late
2020s.

[[Building a new reactor is **uniquely difficult in the United
States**]{.underline} [due to its]{.underline}]{.mark} [own
**self-imposed [bureaucratic straight jacket]{.mark}**]{.underline}[.
[It takes]{.underline}]{.mark} [an average [3.2 years to generate
an]{.mark} environmental impact statement ([EIS]{.mark}) for a new
reactor]{.underline} -- **[that is [generate, not
process]{.mark}]{.underline}**[. [There are]{.underline}]{.mark} [then
[**years** of]{.mark} **additional Nuclear Regulatory Commission
[hearings]{.mark}**]{.underline} [[to]{.mark} officially [license **any
project**]{.mark}]{.underline}. [None of these concern
safety]{.underline} -- safety inspections occur independently of this
process and largely function as designed, lasting only months. [Then,
years of public hearings susceptible and often systematically captured
by private interests and militant environmentalists further delays
construction, **forcing the U.S. to burn coal and natural gas instead of
the clean atom.**]{.underline}

[What was once introduced to prevent government planners from running
roughshod over communities has become a tool of privileged interests to
block construction. This [over-regulation]{.mark} does not protect the
environment and **[deters private investment]{.mark}** in green
energy.]{.underline}

#### [Agency dysfunction]{.underline} prevents effective nuclear leadership

Dr. Rita **Baranwal et al. 24**, Fellow of the American Nuclear Society,

### Leadership\-\--Prolif\-\--No Impact\-\--1NC

#### No prolif\-\--EVEN IF, no impact. 

**Mueller '20** \[John; June 24; senior fellow at the Cato Institute,
member of the political science department and senior research scientist
with the Mershon Center for International Security Studies at Ohio State
University; CATO Institute, "Nuclear Alarmism: Proliferation and
Terrorism,"
https://www.cato.org/publications/publications/nuclear-alarmism-proliferation-terrorism\]

Nuclear Proliferation

In an influential book, Graham Allison argues that "no new nuclear
weapons states" should be a prime foreign policy principle, and analyst
Joseph Cirincione very much agrees, insisting that nonproliferation
should be "our number one national‐​security priority."5

[[There are]{.mark} good [reasons to **avoid
alarmism**]{.mark}]{.underline} in this area, however. First, [the pace
of nuclear [prolif]{.mark}eration [has been]{.mark} **far
[slow]{.mark}er** than has been **commonly predicted** primarily because
the [weapons convey **little advantage**]{.mark} **to their
possessor**]{.underline}. Second, [the **consequences** of such
[prolif]{.mark}eration that **[has]{.mark} taken place** have
[been]{.mark} **substantially [benign]{.mark}**]{.underline}: [those who
have acquired the weapons have "used" them simply to **stoke their
egos** or to **deter**]{.underline} real or imagined
**[threats]{.underline}**.6

And thirdly, the costs of anti‐​proliferation policy have been very
substantial: the number of people who have died as a consequence of
dedicated efforts to contain nuclear proliferation runs well into six
figures.

Pace

Alarmists have been wrong for decades about the pace of nuclear
proliferation. [[**Dozens** of]{.mark} **technologically
[capable]{.mark}** [countries have **considered**]{.mark} obtaining
nuclear [arsenals, but **very few have done
so**]{.mark}**.**]{.underline} Indeed, as Jacques Hymans has pointed
out, even supposedly optimistic forecasts about nuclear dispersion have
proved to be too pessimistic.7 Thus, in 1958, the National Planning
Association predicted "a rapid rise in the number of atomic powers ...
by the mid‐​1960s."8 A few years later, C. P. Snow sagely predicted,
"Within, at the most, six years, China and several other states \[will\]
have a stock of nuclear bombs," and John Kennedy observed that there
might be "ten, fifteen, twenty" countries with a nuclear capacity by
1964.9

As part of that forecasting, it has generally been assumed that nuclear
weapons would be important status --- or virility --- symbols;
therefore, all advanced countries would want to have them in order to
show how "powerful" they were. Thus, France's de Gaulle opined in the
1960s, "No country without an atom bomb could properly consider itself
independent," and Robert Gilpin concluded that "the possession of
nuclear weapons largely determines a nation's rank in the hierarchy of
international prestige."10 In Gilpinian tradition, some analysts who
describe themselves as "realists" have insisted for years that Germany
and Japan must soon come to their senses and quest after nuclear
weapons.11 [Such punditry has gone astray in part because the pundits
insist on **extrapolating** from the **wrong cases**]{.underline}. A
more pertinent prototype would have been [Canada]{.underline}, a country
that **[could]{.underline}** easily [have had nuclear weapons by the
1960s but **declined to make the effort**]{.underline}.12 In fact, over
the decades, [[a **huge number**]{.mark} **of countries** capable of
developing nuclear weapons have **[neglected even to consider the
opportunity]{.mark}** --- for example, Canada, Italy, and Norway ---
even as Argentina, Brazil, Libya, South Korea, and Taiwan have **backed
away from** or **reversed** nuclear weapons programs, and Belarus,
Kazakhstan, South Africa, and Ukraine have actually **surrendered** or
**dismantled** an **existing nuclear arsenal**]{.underline}.13 Some of
that reduction is no doubt due to the hostility of the nuclear nations,
but even without that, the Canadian case seems to have proved to have
rather general relevance.

To begin with, as Stephen Meyer has shown, [there is no "**technological
imperative**" for countries to **obtain nuclear weapons**]{.underline}
once they have achieved the technical capacity to do so.14 Moreover,
like military prowess in general, [the weapons have **not proved** to be
crucial status symbols]{.underline}. As Robert Jervis has observed,
"India, China, and Israel may have decreased the chance of direct attack
by developing nuclear weapons, but it is hard to argue that they have
increased their general prestige or influence."15 How much more status
would Japan have if it possessed nuclear weapons? [Would anybody pay a
**great deal more attention** to Britain or France if their arsenals
held 5,000 nuclear weapons, or would anybody pay **much less** if they
had **none**? Did **China** need nuclear weapons to impress the world
with its **economic growth?**]{.underline} Or with its Olympics? As
Jennifer Mackby and Walter Slocombe observe, "[Germany,
like]{.underline} its erstwhile Axis ally, [[Japan]{.underline}]{.mark},
[has become powerful because of its **economic might** rather than its
**military might**]{.underline}, [and its **[renunciation of
nuc]{.mark}lear weapon[s]{.mark}** may even have **[reinforced]{.mark}
its [prestige]{.mark}**]{.underline}."16

[**[Decades of]{.mark} alarmist [predictions]{.mark}** [about]{.mark}
proliferation **chains**, **[cascades]{.mark}**, **dominoes**,
**waves**, **avalanches**, **epidemics**, and **points of no return**
[have proved]{.mark} to be **[faulty]{.mark}**. The
[proli]{.mark}feration of nuclear weapons [has been]{.mark} **far
[slow]{.mark}er than routinely expected** because, insofar as most

[weapons are **dangerous**]{.mark}, **distasteful**, [**costly**,

Although we have now suffered through two‐​thirds of a century during

[compelling]{.mark}**]{.underline} --- or even minimally sensible ---
**[[reason]{.underline}]{.mark}** [[to **use the
weapons**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [particularly because of an
**inability** to **identify suitable targets**]{.underline} or ones that

#### No spread NOR impact.

The question of whether nuclear proliferation has stabilising or
destabilising effects is not just fascinating for scholars of the
nuclear age, but also highly consequential for practical policy issues.
For in order to debate the merits of particular policy choices -- such
as preventive military strikes against nuclear facilities, grand
bargains with potential proliferators or complete nuclear disarmament --
we need to understand first how the spread of nuclear weapons impacts
regional and global security.

The chapter proceeds in three steps. The first section provides the
foundation for the other parts by summarising what we know about
empirical patterns of proliferation and the utility of nuclear weapons
for statecraft. The second section then engages the literature on the
consequences of proliferation, focusing in particular on how
proliferation has influenced international stability. The final section
explores whether some states have been more affected than others, and
what measures these states have taken to prevent proliferation, or at
least mitigate its negative consequences.

Patterns of nuclear proliferation and the utility of nuclear weapons

Nuclear proliferation is commonly defined as the spread of nuclear
weapons to states that did not previously have them. Within a broader
conceptual framework that is rarely used by scholars, yet popular in the
arms control community, this diffusion of nuclear weapons to additional
states is labelled horizontal proliferation. It is conceptually
accompanied by the notion of vertical proliferation, which refers to
qualitative improvements and increases in the number of nuclear weapons
in the stockpiles of existing nuclear weapon states. In accordance with
the typical usage of the term in the scholarly debate, this chapter
focuses only on how the horizontal proliferation of nuclear weapons
affects international stability.

One important empirical pattern that has shaped how nuclear
proliferation is understood concerns the way in which nuclear weapons
have spread. [The word]{.underline} **[['spread']{.underline}]{.mark}**
[appears to suggest that the **established**]{.underline} nuclear
[powers have]{.underline} [**provide**d]{.underline} [other interested
nations with (at least a few) operational nuclear warheads.]{.underline}
**[Yet such transfers have [never]{.mark} been
[undertaken]{.mark}.]{.underline}** [Certainly, states that sought
nuclear weapons have often received]{.underline} significant
**[assistance]{.underline}** from other nations (Schofield, 2014;
Fuhrmann, 2012), [sometimes in the form of]{.underline} highly sensitive
**[tech]{.underline}**nologies (Kroenig, 2010).
[Nonetheless,]{.underline} since **[all]{.underline}** these transfers
[[remained]{.underline}]{.mark} **[well [below]{.mark}]{.underline}**
[the]{.underline} **[[weapons]{.mark} threshold]{.underline}**, nations
seeking nuclear weapons always had to build them indigenously. Hence,
[in reality, the spread of nuclear weapons has meant that merely the
**ambition**]{.underline} [to possess a nuclear arsenal has
spread]{.underline} to additional states, each of which then had to
pursue that goal primarily through indigenous efforts.

[Importantly, since a state's national [efforts]{.mark} to turn
its]{.underline} **[desire]{.underline}** for nuclear weapons
[into]{.underline} **[reality]{.underline}** [naturally
[span]{.mark}]{.underline} several (and sometimes **[many)
[years]{.mark}]{.underline}**, nuclear **[prolif]{.underline}**eration
[must be conceived of as a]{.underline} **[process]{.underline}**, as
opposed to just a single step (Meyer, 1986). This point is reinforced by
the fact that **[[29]{.underline}]{.mark}** out **[[of
39]{.underline}]{.mark}** states [[that]{.mark} have]{.underline}
**[[embarked]{.underline}]{.mark}** [upon that path]{.underline} (Müller

the technological capability to build an arsenal quickly while
refraining from exercising that option (Narang, 2016--17, p. 134).

nuclear explosive devices (Müller and Schmidt, 2010).

#### Even if ambitions are immediately triggered, the impact is slow and reversible

**Schneider '19** \[Jonas; 2019; Center for Security Studies;
Understanding Global Politics: Actors and Themes in International
Affairs, "Nuclear Proliferation and International Security," Ch. 26\]

Other analysts have sounded a much less alarmist tone, however. Some
scholars even suggested that an Iranian bomb held great potential for
stabilising an unbalanced and volatile Middle East (Waltz, 2012). Closer
to the mainstream of Western strategic discourse, various experts have
argued that despite the risks of proliferation, nuclear weapons, and the
deterrent they provide should get (more) credit for contributing, in
combination with other factors, to what has been labelled 'the Long
Peace' among the great powers since 1945 (Gaddis, 1999, p. 268--271;
Gavin, 2012a, p. 164; Acton 2010, pp. 16--17). Still others have
contended that because nuclear proliferation is such a rare phenomenon,
and since robust nonproliferation measures tend to be disruptive, the
net destabilising effect of new nuclear countries is quite small and,
therefore, manageable (Mueller 2010, pp. 95--99; Hymans 2013, pp.
293--296). The question of whether nuclear proliferation has stabilising
or destabilising effects is not just fascinating for scholars of the
nuclear age, but also highly consequential for practical policy issues.
For in order to debate the merits of particular policy choices -- such
as preventive military strikes against nuclear facilities, grand
bargains with potential proliferators or complete nuclear disarmament --
we need to understand first how the spread of nuclear weapons impacts
regional and global security. The chapter proceeds in three steps. The
first section provides the foundation for the other parts by summarising
what we know about empirical patterns of proliferation and the utility
of nuclear weapons for statecraft. The second section then engages the
literature on the consequences of proliferation, focusing in particular
on how proliferation has influenced international stability. The final
section explores whether some states have been more affected than
others, and what measures these states have taken to prevent
proliferation, or at least mitigate its negative consequences. Patterns
of nuclear proliferation and the utility of nuclear weapons Nuclear
proliferation is commonly defined as the spread of nuclear weapons to
states that did not previously have them. Within a broader conceptual
framework that is rarely used by scholars, yet popular in the arms
control community, this diffusion of nuclear weapons to additional
states is labelled horizontal proliferation. It is conceptually
accompanied by the notion of vertical proliferation, which refers to
qualitative improvements and increases in the number of nuclear weapons
in the stockpiles of existing nuclear weapon states. In accordance with
the typical usage of the term in the scholarly debate, this chapter
focuses only on how the horizontal proliferation of nuclear weapons
affects international stability. One important empirical pattern that
has shaped how nuclear proliferation is understood concerns the way in
which nuclear weapons have spread. The word 'spread' appears to suggest
that the established nuclear powers have provided other interested
nations with (at least a few) operational nuclear warheads. Yet such
transfers have never been undertaken. Certainly, states that sought
nuclear weapons have often received significant assistance from other
nations (Schofield, 2014; Fuhrmann, 2012), sometimes in the form of
highly sensitive technologies (Kroenig, 2010). Nonetheless, since all
these transfers remained well below the weapons threshold, nations
seeking nuclear weapons always had to build them indigenously. Hence,
[in reality, the [spread]{.mark} of nuclear weapons [has meant that
merely]{.mark} the **[ambition]{.mark}**]{.underline} [to possess a

The judgment of scholars has been scathing. Yusuf concludes that "an

WASHINGTON, D.C. -- The [U.S. **D**epartment **o**f
**E**nergy]{.underline} (DOE) [today re-issued a **\$900 million

**r**eactors to better align with President **Trump\'s** bold agenda to
**unleash American energy** and AI dominance.]{.underline}

**m**odular **r**eactors]{.underline}."

U.S. electricity demand is forecast to soar in the coming years driven
by consumer needs, data center growth, increased AI use, and the
industrial sector's need for constant power.

Small modular reactors could provide reliable power for these
energy-intensive sectors, with the added benefit of flexible deployment
thanks to their compact size and modular design. Light-water small
modular reactors could also leverage the existing service and supply
chain supporting the country's current fleet of light-water reactors,
helping speed up the near-term deployment of new nuclear reactors.

[**DOE is offering funding** to de-risk the deployment of Generation
III+ light-water small modular reactors]{.underline} (Gen III+ SMR)

as design, licensing, supply chain, and site preparation]{.underline}.

## AT: Readiness ADV

### Readiness\-\--SMRs Bad\-\--1NC

#### SMRs [undermine]{.underline} resilience\-\--status quo measures [solve]{.underline}.

**Kuperman '24** \[Alan J.; October 7; Associate Professor of Public
Affairs @ U.T. Austin, Ph.D. in Political Science from MIT, M.A. in
International Relations and International Economics at JHU, A.B. in
Physical Sciences from Harvard, Founder of Nuclear Proliferation
Prevention Project; Breaking Defense, "On Army bases, nuclear energy
can't add resilience, just costs and risks,"
https://breakingdefense.com/2024/10/on-army-bases-nuclear-energy-cant-add-resilience-just-costs-and-risks/\]
\-\--pancakes!

Every now and then, the US government offers a huge subsidy to an
industry on grounds that make no sense to anyone with even basic
knowledge of the subject. The latest example, announced in June, is the
Army's Advanced Nuclear Power for Installations (ANPI) [program to
install **small reactors** on military bases]{.underline}, ostensibly
[to increase "energy **resilience**]{.underline}."

This [is **perplexing**]{.underline} for several reasons. First, such
[resilience can be provided much more **effectively**, **safely**, and
**cheaply** with **non-nuclear**]{.underline} options. Second, nuclear
reactors [themselves cannot provide "resilience]{.underline}," because
[their **safe** operation]{.underline} always has [require]{.underline}d
input of [electricity]{.underline} to the reactors [from
**other**]{.underline} power [sources]{.underline}. Third,
[the]{.underline} Army's planned [reactors would lack]{.underline} a
[robust **containment**]{.underline} building, [so an **attack** or
**accident** could disperse **radioactive waste**]{.underline},
endangering base personnel and neighboring civilians.

Both the Army and taxpayers should cry foul on this indefensible waste
of national security dollars.

Of course, energy resilience is a reasonable concern for Army bases,
which now get their electricity from the commercial [grid]{.underline}
that [is]{.underline} potentially [subject to **blackouts**]{.underline}
from bad weather or even cyberattacks. [The **simple** and
**inexpensive** solution]{.underline}, [**already** utilized by military
bases]{.underline} and other essential services including hospitals, [is
to maintain **backup**]{.underline} diesel [fuel and
generators]{.underline} for emergency use. It costs only about \$2
million to \$4 million for a set of diesel generators to produce 5
megawatts of electricity --- the amount the Army seeks --- and the
[diesel fuel would be **cheap** since]{.underline} the
[generators]{.underline} would operate [only during **rare**
emergencies]{.underline}.

By contrast, the [price of a single nuclear reactor]{.underline} to
produce the same five megawatts of electricity would be several hundred
million dollars --- roughly [**100** times as expensive]{.underline} ---
[according to government estimates]{.underline} and my previously
published research. Even if, as the Army hopes, the reactor could
replace the commercial grid as the primary source of power for the base,
the electricity produced by the reactor would cost several times more
than what the Army now pays for commercial electricity. So, regardless
of whether the reactor was used for primary or backup power, [Army costs
would **spike**]{.underline} substantially.

What about resilience, which is the supposed justification for buying
these expensive reactors? Well, even though reactors can produce
electricity, they have always required an external source of electricity
to keep them running safely --- most crucially to cool the fuel to avoid
a nuclear meltdown and radioactive release. The Army's recent request
for proposals seems to acknowledge this reality by saying that in
addition to an external electricity source, the reactor must have an
"alternative credited independent power source as a backup."

Therefore, an Army base reactor would almost surely depend on drawing
electricity from the commercial grid. But this means the reactor would
be no more resilient than the existing power source it is supposed to
replace to increase resilience. [In the event of a blackout]{.underline}
of the commercial grid, what would [the reactor]{.underline} do to get
essential electricity? Of course, it [would turn on its backup
**diesel** generators]{.underline}. However, if the base requires backup
generators anyway, it has [no need for the **super-expensive**
reactor]{.underline}.

It gets even worse. [To prevent **costs** from rising]{.underline} even
higher, [the nuclear industry]{.underline} has [decided]{.underline}
that its [small reactors]{.underline} --- the kind the Army is seeking
--- [will be built **without** a containment building that]{.underline}
could [**prevent** radiation from escaping]{.underline} in the event of
an accident. This also [means the reactors would be more **vulnerable**
to attack]{.underline} by aircraft, missiles, rockets, and drones.

A successful kinetic attack could spread radioactivity in at least two
ways. First, like a "dirty bomb," it could [disperse]{.underline} the
reactor's solid [irradiated fuel]{.underline} over a wide area into a
few [or]{.underline} many [**radioactive** chunks]{.underline} that
would be very hazardous if approached. Even worse, if the attack
interrupted the reactor's active or passive cooling, the fuel could
overheat and breach its cladding, thereby allowing gaseous radioactivity
to spread more widely.

### Readiness\-\--No Solvency\-\--1NC

#### SMRs can't end reliance on [convoys]{.underline}. Status quo [solves]{.underline} resilience. 

**Kuperman '21** \[Alan J.; April 22; Associate Professor of Public
Affairs @ U.T. Austin, Ph.D. in Political Science from MIT, M.A. in
International Relations and International Economics at JHU, A.B. in
Physical Sciences from Harvard, Founder of Nuclear Proliferation
Prevention Project; Nuclear Proliferation Prevention Project , "Proposed
U.S. Army Mobile Nuclear Reactors: Costs and Risks Outweigh Benefits,"
p. 3-5\] \-\--pancakes!\
Rationale #1: Casualty Reduction\
The [**main** rationale]{.underline} cited [to justify]{.underline} Army
**[mobile reactors]{.underline}** for FOBs [is to reduce **casualties**
from **convoys** that deliver **diesel** fuel for **generators** to
produce electricity]{.underline}, a risk typically illustrated by a
stock photograph (Figure 4). [The statistical evidence for
this]{.underline} rationale [is]{.underline} quite
**[old]{.underline}**, [dating to 2010]{.underline}, as cited in a 2015
RAND report: "From October 2001 to December 2010, of the approximately
36,000 total U.S. casualties in OIF \[Iraq\] and OEF \[Afghanistan\],
about 18,700 (52 percent) occurred from hostile attacks during land
transport missions."38 This outdated statistic is still cited in recent
government documents including the 2018 Army report, a 2018 Energy
Department report, and a 2021 Army presentation.39 However,
[the]{.underline} historical [claim cannot be **verified**]{.underline},
because it is based on a Pentagon study that is "not available to the
general public."40\
Figure 4. Diesel fuel convoy to forward operating base\
Source: Michael E. Canes, "Managing Military Energy for Greater Cost
Effectiveness," November 6, 2012.\
[**Contradictory** evidence]{.underline} is [provided in]{.underline} a
publicly available 2009 [Army study]{.underline} that [cites a **much
lower** casualty rate]{.underline} from convoys, as follows: "Resupply
casualties have been significant in Iraq and Afghanistan. According to
the Center for Army Lessons Learned, they have historically accounted
for about 10-12% of total Army casualties -- the majority related to
fuel and water transport."41\
Even if the 2010 statistic were accurate, it would not justify MNPPs for
several reasons. First, according to the Army, only about
**[half]{.underline}** of the cited land convoys were for fuel, while
the rest [were for **water**]{.underline} and other supplies.42 Second,
according to a 2009 GAO study of five FOBs in June 2008, an average of
[65 percent of]{.underline} the [diesel]{.underline} fuel delivered [was
not for]{.underline} electricity [generation]{.underline} but rather for
land vehicles and aircraft.43 This evidence suggests that only about 18
percent (.5 x .35) of the convoys comprised diesel for electricity
generation. Thus, even if the 2010 statistic were correct that 52
percent of casualties had been from convoys, and assuming no bias in the
distribution of convoy attacks or resulting casualties, only nine
percent (.18 x .52) of casualties during this period ending a decade ago
were attributable to convoys of diesel for electricity generation.
Moreover, [the share of]{.underline} delivered [diesel fuel]{.underline}
that is [used for electricity generation is]{.underline} even
**[small]{.underline}**er than 35 percent at big bases, which are the
main candidates for MNPPs. Thus, [mobile reactors]{.underline} at best
[could eliminate only a **small** fraction]{.underline} of the
casualties associated with a war base.\
The 2010 statistic is further misleading because during the past decade
the incidence of casualties per convoy has plummeted dramatically.
Figures 5 and 6 show that annual military deaths in Afghanistan and Iraq
dropped sharply from 2007 to 2019, even as fuel deliveries increased, so
the ratio of military deaths to delivered fuel decreased by 25fold. In
2005, there were 4.7 military deaths per million gallons of fuel used,
but nine years later this ratio had declined dramatically to 0.19,
despite similar amounts of fuel being used. This evidence indicates
that, by 2014, the [Pentagon had largely **solved**]{.underline} the
[problem]{.underline} of U.S. casualties [arising from fuel
convoys]{.underline}, apparently [through several
**innovations**]{.underline}: [better **roads**]{.underline}, an
improved relationship with Pakistan, [**outsourcing**
transport]{.underline} to non-U.S. personnel, [and use of
**airlift**]{.underline} where land routes were especially vulnerable.44
This finding is not an artifact of troop withdrawal because the largest
decrease in the casualty rate, from 2007 to 2009, occurred during an
increase in the number of deployed forces (Figure 7).\
Figure 5. Annual U.S. fuel use and military deaths in Afghanistan and
Iraq\
Sources: See Appendix 1.\
It is not known why the Army, in its 2018 report, cited an outdated
casualty ratio that was 25 times higher than the reality at the time,
but this misleading statistic cannot be used to justify mobile nuclear
reactors. Even if convoys still were a large cause of U.S. troop
deaths[, it is **unlikely** that the most **efficient** way to
reduce]{.underline} such [casualties would be]{.underline} to develop,
manufacture, and deploy [**expensive** nuclear reactors that could
eliminate only a **fraction** of the]{.underline} 18 percent of
[convoys]{.underline} that carry diesel for electricity generation. As a
2016 scholarly article concluded, "Since water and waste trucks are
exposed to similar risks and subject to similar costs as fuel trucks ...
it is possible that [much **greater** benefits]{.underline} with much
less investment [can be achieved by targeting **water** and
**waste**]{.underline} logistics volumes instead of fuel."45

### Readiness\-\--No Solvency\-\--2NC

#### SMRs aren't more [resilient]{.underline}\-\--they still have to rely on [external]{.underline} sources of power.

**Lyman '24** \[Ed; April 30; Director of Nuclear Power Safety with the
Union of Concerned Scientists, PhD in Physics from Cornell University;
Union of Concerned Scientists, "Five Things the 'Nuclear Bros' Don't
Want You to Know About Small Modular Reactors,"
https://blog.ucs.org/edwin-lyman/five-things-the-nuclear-bros-dont-want-you-to-know-about-small-modular-reactors/\]

Despite the claims of developers, [it is **very unlikely**
that]{.underline} any reasonably foreseeable [SMR]{.underline} design
[would]{.underline} be able to [**safely** operate without]{.underline}
reliable [access to]{.underline} electricity from [the **grid** to power
coolant pumps and]{.underline} other [vital **safety**
systems]{.underline}. Just like today's nuclear plants, [SMRs will be
**vulnerable** to extreme **weather** events or other **disasters**
that]{.underline} could [cause a loss of **offsite** power and force
them to **shut down**]{.underline}. In such situations a user such as a
data center operator [would have to provide **backup**
power]{.underline}, likely [from **diesel** generators]{.underline}, for
both the data center AND the reactor. And since there is virtually no
experience with operating SMRs worldwide, it is highly doubtful that the
novel designs being pitched now would be highly reliable right out of
the box and require little monitoring and maintenance.

[It]{.underline} very likely [will take **decades**]{.underline} of
operating experience [for **any**]{.underline} new [reactor design to
achieve]{.underline} the level of [**[reliability]{.mark}**]{.underline}
characteristic [of the operating **light-water**]{.underline} reactor
[fleet]{.underline}. Premature deployment based on unrealistic
performance expectations could prove extremely costly for any company
that wants to experiment with SMRs.

**[Climate change]{.underline}** and its [**direct** and **indirect**
effects]{.underline}, including [on **politics** and
**security**]{.underline}, also [pose **risks** for nuclear power
plants]{.underline} and other critical infrastructure. Direct effects
include rapid-onset [extreme **weather** events]{.underline} (such as
**[storms]{.underline}** and storm surges, [heatwaves and flash
**floods**]{.underline}) [as well as]{.underline} slower-onset phenomena
(such as [**sea-level** rise]{.underline}, [**water**
scarcity]{.underline}, [changes to]{.underline} rainfall or average
**[temperatures]{.underline}**). All of this can
[**undermine**]{.underline} the safe and [secure **functioning** of
nuclear facilities]{.underline}. For instance,
**[drought]{.underline}**---especially compounded by competing demands
for water---could [disrupt the **cooling water** supply]{.underline} to
a reactor, potentially [necessitating a **shutdown**]{.underline}, while
[floods or storms could damage **critical** systems]{.underline}.\
A 2021 analysis of nuclear power plants' vulnerability to such
climate-linked effects is full of important insights. The [study
shows]{.underline} that the average frequency of [**climate-linked**
power outages]{.underline} at nuclear power plants globally has
[**dramatically** increased]{.underline}---from 0.2 outages per
reactor-year in the 1990s to about 0.82 in 2000s, to 1.5 in 2010--19. It
projects that [energy losses]{.underline} due to climate change [will
continue to rise]{.underline} among the world's nuclear power plants.\
That study's findings for the period 2010--19 suggest that, after
hurricanes and typhoons (in the United States, South and South East
Asia), the second largest climate-linked contributor to outages was
increased [ambient temperatures]{.underline}. Higher ambient
temperatures can affect nuclear power plants in a variety of ways. For
example, warmer temperatures [can foster the]{.underline} rapid [growth
of **algae** or other **biological** material]{.underline}, which can in
turn [**clog** cooling water intakes]{.underline}, [reducing
**production** and]{.underline} even [requiring]{.underline} the [plant
to **shut down**]{.underline}, particularly in warmer areas or reactors
using seawater for cooling. High ambient temperatures can also [make
power generation **less efficient**]{.underline}. Many of the developing
countries where electricity access is currently lowest (and thus where
SMRs might have the greatest value in promoting development) already
have relatively high ambient temperatures, which are likely to increase
further.

### Readiness\-\--Squo Solves\-\--1NC

#### [Efficiency]{.underline} upgrades mean SMRs are deployed at a [small]{.underline} number of [rear-deployed]{.underline} bases that already have a [resilient]{.underline} energy supply.

**Kuperman '21** \[Alan J.; April 22; Associate Professor of Public
Affairs @ U.T. Austin, Ph.D. in Political Science from MIT, M.A. in
International Relations and International Economics at JHU, A.B. in
Physical Sciences from Harvard, Founder of Nuclear Proliferation
Prevention Project; Nuclear Proliferation Prevention Project , "Proposed
U.S. Army Mobile Nuclear Reactors: Costs and Risks Outweigh Benefits,"
p. 3-5\] \-\--pancakes!\
Few Suitable FOBs\
Even proponents of MNPPs concede that manufacturing the first one would
be expensive, but they argue that per-unit costs would decline sharply
as large numbers were ordered. In reality, however, there will be
[**few** if any FOBs that use **enough** electricity to]{.underline}
potentially [justify a MNPP]{.underline}, so it is unlikely that
economies of scale from mass production could be realized. The Pele
Program RFS specified the power output of MNPPs as ranging from 1 to 10
MWe, but as noted the maximum apparently was subsequently capped at 5
MWe. The cost per unit of power would likely decline with increasing
size, so **[low-power]{.underline}** MNPPs with output of 1 MWe [would
be **least** economical]{.underline}. If casualties from convoys of
diesel ever again became a problem, the lower power MNPPs also would do
less to address it, since they could eliminate fewer convoys. Therefore,
[the **strongest** possible case]{.underline} for MNPPs can be [made for
**larger**]{.underline} [ones]{.underline} with maximum output [of
either **5 or 10 MWe**]{.underline}. This raises a key question: How
many FOBs use so much electricity?\
Electricity demand and supply at a FOB typically correlate positively
with number of personnel, but not necessarily linearly. [Small FOBs
have]{.underline} few amenities and thus [**low** electricity
demand]{.underline} per capita. They also tend to be inefficient in
producing electricity, because they simultaneously run multiple
generators well below capacity in the absence of a microgrid. Larger
FOBs have a broader range of activities and amenities, and thus higher
electricity demand per capita. They also can produce electricity more
efficiently by operating prime generators at or near capacity for
distribution via a microgrid, although in practice they also operate
many smaller generators less efficiently. Usage of electricity also
varies within and between days, so that in the absence of energy storage
the production capacity must exceed not only the average usage but the
peak demand.\
Table 1. Energy Usage at Afghanistan FOBs: 2010 Per Capita Estimates\
Source: Michael Bowes and Barry Pifer, "Reducing Energy Footprint on the
Battlefield," CNA, CRM D0022638.A2/Final, June 2010, 17-19. Note: gpd =
gallons of diesel fuel per day (per capita).\
Previous research suggests that at large FOBs, the per capita
electricity demand is about 1 kW average and 2 kW peak, and the supply
capacity is larger. A 2010 study of Marine Corps FOBs in Afghanistan by
CNA (Table 1) found that such camps, which typically house at least
5,000 personnel, had average demand of 1.0 to 1.2 kW, which was supplied
by a capacity of 4 kW per person. Exemplifying this, in August 2009,
Camp Leatherneck in Afghanistan housed about 5,000 Marines and had an
average electricity demand of 5 MW, supplied by 196 generators with
total capacity of 19 MW,66 indicating that the electricity capacity was
about four times the average demand. Army test base studies suggest that
peak usage is typically about twice average usage,67 so Leatherneck's
peak usage may have been around 10 MW, or 2 kW per person.68\
Electricity supply capacity on FOBs often dwarfs both average and peak
demand due to inefficiencies, as at Camp Leatherneck in 2009, but the
Pentagon is working to fix that in two main ways. First, microgrids
allow electricity from any generator to be channeled to any end user, so
that supply capacity can be reduced to slightly above peak demand.
Second, energy storage allows electricity produced at times of low usage
to be utilized at times of high usage, so that supply capacity can be
reduced further to slightly above average demand. This suggests [a FOB
with **5,000** personnel could be **adequately** supplied]{.underline}
by 5 to 10 MWe of electricity generation, depending on the extent of the
microgrid and energy storage.\
[Assuming]{.underline} that Pele [MNPPs]{.underline} would be
**[most]{.underline}** cost [efficient]{.underline} if they produced
[at]{.underline} the **[maximum]{.underline}** [power]{.underline} level
(5 to 10 MWe), and that such output would suffice for 5,000 personnel,
it is necessary to explore [how many U.S. FOBs have]{.underline} at
least [that population]{.underline}. According to a RAND analysis, from
2012 to 2014, when the U.S. averaged 60,000 troops in Afghanistan, six
of its bases each exceeded 3,000 personnel on average, including
contractors.69 By extrapolation, a very rough estimate is that
[at]{.underline} the **[peak]{.underline}** of US
[deployment]{.underline} to Afghanistan and Iraq, in 2009, when slightly
over 200,000 troops were deployed, [about a **dozen**]{.underline} bases
contained 5,000 or more personnel and thus potentially could have been
suitable for the most economic utilization of one or more MNPPs.70
Today, by contrast, the number of such FOBs is zero, which would inhibit
the economic utilization of MNPPs. Prospectively, as well, [in light of
**diminished**]{.underline} U.S. public support for [large
deployments]{.underline} of forces, **[few]{.underline}** if any
cost-effective [opportunities]{.underline} for MNPPs on FOBs may arise
in the foreseeable future.\
The [Pentagon]{.underline} also is [**cutting** electricity
capacity]{.underline} needs on FOBs [through **efficiency**
reforms]{.underline} such as insulating barracks and equipping them with
thermostats, and adding microgrids and energy storage.71 The Army has
found such changes can [cut by **more than half** the **diesel** fuel
required]{.underline} for electricity at a large base in Afghanistan by
diminishing the demand for electricity and increasing the efficiency of
its production.72 Renewable energy, including solar and wind, can help
decrease the remaining demand for non-renewable sources.73 A U.S.
national laboratory also has explored connecting FOBs to the power grids
of their host countries,74 as is typical for other types of overseas
U.S. bases.75 [Such reforms]{.underline} will **[reduce]{.underline}**
further the [likelihood]{.underline} that a future [FOB would
**require** 5 to 10 MWe]{.underline} of power from a MNPP, as necessary
to optimize the reactor's economics. In light of this reality, the Army
in 2018 modified its rhetoric, claiming instead that [MNPPs]{.underline}
could be [deployed to large **rear** bases]{.underline} -- rather than
forward bases in war zones. However, this switch undermined the Army's
ostensible main rationale for expensive MNPPs: to reduce casualties from
delivering diesel fuel to FOBs. The rear bases that the Army identifies
as candidates for MNPPs are located in secure locations such as Alaska
and Puerto Rico,76 which face little risk of casualties from fuel
deliveries. Such bases [already have **secure** and affordable
electricity]{.underline} supply, typically either from the local power
grid or generators utilizing diesel fuel that can be delivered by
surface vessels without armed escort, thereby sharply reducing the fully
burdened cost of fuel and energy.77

### Airpower\-\--Impact Defense\-\--1NC

#### Air power [doesn't]{.underline} stop war\-\--if anything, it makes conflict [more likely]{.underline}.

### Airpower\-\--Impact Defense\-\--2NC

#### It is [especially insignificant]{.underline} with regards to [peer adversaries]{.underline}\-\--AND, our [overreliance]{.underline} makes warfighting and readiness [worse]{.underline}.

\-\--ISTAR=intelligence, surveillance, target acquisition, and

Justin **Bronk 21**, RUSI Research Fellow for Airpower and Technology,

Thanks, Peter, I think [[the]{.mark} overarching [thing
that]{.mark}]{.underline}, sort of, **[[sticks out for

[success of]{.underline}, basically [the US Air Force and the coalition
in clearing the skies and then really smashing the Iraqi forces in 1991

[there was a desire to keep **footprints to a minimum on the

Pacific theatre like South Korea, for example, that [[we have,
really]{.mark}, **very, [very limited ground and maritime-based
firepower]{.mark}**]{.underline}[.]{.mark} [[There is this **huge
dependency on**]{.mark} **the [air]{.mark} side**]{.underline}, [[which
becomes **very**]{.mark}**, very [problematic]{.mark}**]{.underline}
[[if you then need to **plan to or**]{.mark}**, indeed, god forbid,
[actually fight]{.mark}**]{.underline} [[an **opponent**]{.mark} who can
make that air superiority very **difficult to achieve.**]{.underline}

#### Air power doesn't change [overall]{.underline} balance of power.

Dr. Jeremy **Black 16**, PhD from Durham University, Professor and
Established Chair in History at Exeter University, "Air Power: Strength
and Weaknesses",
https://defenceindepth.co/2016/05/11/air-power-strength-and-weaknesses/

These issues raise questions not only about how best to present the
history of air power, but also concerning its past and continuing
rationale and relevance. [[Air power]{.mark},]{.underline} especially if
missiles are included, [[has always appealed to those]{.mark} who are
**[looking for "the modern,"]{.mark}**]{.underline} [[the]{.underline}
**["latest and the greatest,"]{.underline}**]{.mark} [and for a
strategic **"magic bullet."**]{.underline} However, [the
[accounts]{.mark} of contemporaries [about
**effectiveness**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [and the hopes of the advocates
[of air power, have frequently proved
**misplaced**]{.mark}]{.underline}, and often seriously so. [This is the
case both for the **military outcomes** of its use and for their
**political consequences.**]{.underline}

Nevertheless, despite the problems confronted in adapting circumstances,
learning lessons, coping with the pressures of commitments, and
responding to fiscal exigencies, air power has dramatically changed
equations for firepower and mobility. More specifically, both in its own
right, and as part of combined air operations, air power has made
manoeuvre warfare a more central part of conflict. As a result, air
power has greatly increased the tempo of war as well as its potential
deadliness.

As with armoured warfare when it was introduced in the 1910s, the
perception of the capability of air power and its reality were very
different. This was also true for fears of what it might mean for
warfare. [There is generally a **poor understanding** of the
**reliability** of aircraft systems]{.underline}. In practice, [the more
complex a system, the less reliable it is.]{.underline} [And there is
the issue of appropriate use]{.underline}. Thus, [[air power is **not a
panacea**]{.underline}]{.mark}. However, in one particular respect, air
power fulfilled the hopes of some early advocates. Thanks to the
successful integration of reconnaissance information with artillery,
aircraft helped overcome the relative stasis of First World War land
operations. In doing so, aircraft helped restore mobility and, at least,
a sense of results to ground operations, although this achievement was
heavily qualified in terms of outcomes. In the 1918 Allied victory on
the Western Front, more was due to the effective use of artillery
incorporating the advantages of air-derived information than to new
weapons, whether tanks or aircraft, operating in ground support roles,
let alone to long-range bombing. Nevertheless, air power indeed proved
part of the equation in translating advantages into the ability to
defeat opposing forces on the ground, as it also did in 1918 with the
British success against the Turks in Palestine.

This factor remained important in the understanding, presentation and
use of air power. All of the combatants in the Second World War believed
in the value of air dominance or supremacy and in its impact on
operations on the ground, even if not all pursued the latter with the
immediacy understood by the term ground support. Indeed, the stress on
the war-winning dimension of air power encouraged Britain and the United
States, neither of which saw their army as war-winning, to focus not on
ground support but on gaining an air dominance that could be used for
strategic bombing campaigns against their opponents' home countries,
rather than, at least as primarily intended, to affect operations on the
ground. However, the latter was also an objective, even if the
relationship between strategic bombing and theatre dominance was
frequently somewhat unclear in practice.

These goals and priorities were not static. They were affected by
resources, opportunities, doctrine and the ability to respond. Thus, the
American ability to demonstrate flexibility and rethink the situation,
and to plan and produce accordingly, led to the development and use of a
long-range escort fighter capability. Similarly, in large part in
response to Japanese advances in China and Japanese advances in the
Pacific, the Americans moved their focus for air attacks on Japan from
China-based aircraft to those operating from Pacific bases, and this had
significant strategic and operational consequences. Ground-support
proved more significant from the outset for militaries that were reliant
on their armies for war-winning, rather than on strategic bombing. This
was the case in the Second World War for Germany, Japan, the Soviet
Union and China.

The demonstration of air power in the 1940s challenged the traditional
geopolitical dichotomy of land and sea. Contemporaries, such as Carl
Schmidt in Land and Sea (1942), argued that this would revolutionize
geopolitics. The range and tempo of geopolitical rivalry was certainly
different. During the Cold War, contrasting national legacies,
priorities and opportunities very much affected the protagonists. The
United States and Britain continued to place an emphasis on strategic
bombing, one greatly enhanced by the availability of nuclear weapons. In
contrast, although the Soviet Union had effective long-range bombers, as
it had not done during the Second World War, the stress there was on
ground-support.

[As of the present, [air power has]{.mark}]{.underline}
[**[confirmed]{.underline}**, **[not challenged]{.underline}**]{.mark},
[the [overall]{.mark} ranking of [military

has a great potential. Air power therefore very much continues to be

### Airpower Bad\-\--1NC

#### Overreliance on air power is [bad]{.underline}\-\--it [trades off]{.underline} with land power, undermines [state stability]{.underline}, causes [terrorism]{.underline}, and [weakens deterrence]{.underline}.

Dr. Steven **Metz 16**, PhD in Political Science from Johns Hopkins
University, Senior Research Professor and the Henry L. Stimson Professor
of Military Studies at the U.S. Army War College Strategic Studies
Institute, "For U.S., Ignoring the Limitations of Airpower a Recipe for
Disaster",
https://www.worldpoliticsreview.com/for-u-s-ignoring-the-limitations-of-airpower-a-recipe-for-disaster/?one-time-read-code=35983166440707848432

There are good reasons for this. [[Relying on airpower]{.underline},
**[rather than direct methods]{.underline}**]{.mark} [that put U.S.
troops in close proximity to enemies]{.underline}, [lowers American
casualties]{.underline}. [Airpower exploits America's technological
superiority]{.underline}. [And it's]{.underline}
\[[[is]{.underline}]{.mark}\] [[easier to disengage]{.mark}, should
things go badly]{.underline}, if the United States does not have troops
on the ground. Aircraft can fly away from a losing cause without seeming
to retreat or surrender.

Because of this, [airpower has long been a **foundation** of American
military power]{.underline}. **[[The problem,]{.underline}]{.mark}**
[though, [is that U.S. airpower is so **good**]{.mark} **at what it
does** accomplish [that policymakers]{.mark} and the public [can **lose

to seek]{.mark} the sort of [decisive results that only come from a
**direct** ]{.mark}**application of [force]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [[The
result is]{.mark} endless **frustration**]{.underline}, [which in turn
**fuels [calls for U.S. disengagement from the
world]{.mark}**]{.underline}. To avoid this vicious cycle, American
military leaders, policymakers, political leaders and opinion-shapers
must be clear on what airpower can and can't do.

For instance, airpower is very good at denying an opponent that relies
on conventional military force a decisive victory. No conventional
military---or even a semi-conventional one, like the self-declared
Islamic State force that captured Mosul and other Iraqi cities last
year---can withstand the precise and withering fire delivered by
American airpower. This makes it a powerful and vital deterrent of
conventional aggression and component of containment. Airpower can
salvage an unraveling situation when a partner is collapsing under
military pressure. And airpower can even things up when the United
States is backing an irregular force against a conventional military,
such as Afghanistan's Northern Alliance in its war with the Taliban
after the attacks of 9/11.

[What [airpower **can't**]{.mark} **do**]{.underline}, though, [is
[defeat an **irregular opponent**]{.mark}]{.underline} [in the absence
of an **effective ground force**]{.underline}, whether American, local
or some combination of the two. This is because, [in the face of intense
air attacks]{.underline}, [[an irregular force can
**disperse**]{.underline}, **[go to ground]{.underline}**,
[and]{.underline} **[wait out the storm]{.underline}**]{.mark}, [then
**reconstitute** and **resume operations**]{.underline}. [Nor can air

[[or]{.underline} **[stabilize a post-conflict state]{.underline}** [to

[airpower is masterful at **destroying**]{.mark} **targets** and enemy

war.]{.mark}**]{.underline}

To put this in today's context, airpower can prevent the Islamic State
from taking more Iraqi cities. It can deter outright, large-scale
military aggression by China, Russia, Iran or North Korea. But it cannot
defeat the Islamic State, stabilize North Korea after a war, or prevent
the kind of camouflaged aggression below the threshold for war that
Russia has used against its neighbors and China is using to seize
control of the South China Sea.

But are these latter missions vital to U.S. national security, or even
important? The United States could alter its global strategy to one
based exclusively on indirect military power, including seapower,
Special Operations Forces activities, and airpower. As Mike Pietrucha
and Jeremy Renken [put
it](http://warontherocks.com/2015/08/airpower-may-not-win-wars-but-it-sure-doesnt-lose-them/),
"not all conflicts have to be resolved by the seizure of land, and not
all political solutions require armed occupations to enforce."

But some do.

**[[The sort of airpower-centric strategy]{.underline}]{.mark}** [which
Pietrucha and Renken recommend]{.underline} would [only work
