general [contended ](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=JY8KjKNIh24)that
American airpower]{.underline}---properly applied to achieve certain
effects---[could have quickly destroyed the Islamic State in Iraq and
Syria in about four months.]{.underline} **[[Airpower-centric
arguments]{.underline}]{.mark}** like this [[do not grapple with
the]{.mark} **thorny [questions]{.mark}** [of **what comes
after**]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark}

Such [airpower advocacy does not just emanate from Air Force
circles]{.underline}. Retired US Army Gen. David [Petraeus
and]{.underline} Michael [O'Hanlon penned a 2016 op-ed, "[It's time to
unleash America's airpower in
Afghanistan](https://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/its-time-to-unleash-americas-air-power-in-afghanistan/2016/01/14/a9c3bd42-b979-11e5-99f3-184bc379b12d_story.html?utm_term=.7f9439ca3b15),"]{.underline}
and followed it up months later with another piece, "[Take the Gloves
Off Against the
Taliban](https://www.wsj.com/articles/take-the-gloves-off-against-the-taliban-1463783106),"
where they reinforce their call for a shift towards more airpower. [Such
arguments rest on rationalistic assumptions about a ground adversary
being willing to expose their foot soldiers, vehicles, and bases for
opportune aerial bombing]{.underline}. However, **[these expectations
miss the reality that [an enemy]{.mark}]{.underline}**---[[and a **local
population**]{.underline}]{.mark}---[[will **not acquiesce**]{.mark}
**solely**]{.underline} [[to the **application of kinetic
airpower**]{.underline}]{.mark} and a limited number of ground troops.
**[Larger numbers of [ground forces]{.mark}]{.underline}**
[[are]{.underline} [needed]{.underline}]{.mark} [to maintain an **active
presence**]{.underline}---day and night---[if counterinsurgents are to
overcome the problem of **locals being intimidated**]{.underline}
[and]{.underline} **[coerced by insurgents]{.underline}**. In fact, many
American troops will tell you that the Taliban prefer to stage attacks
on days with cloud cover or inclement weather, since Taliban fighters
know they can achieve near parity in a ground fight without concern that
an A-10, drone, or attack helicopter will show up to provide air cover
and deny their assault.

More broadly, [what many of these **airpower arguments** miss about the
**evolution of warfare**]{.underline}---especially since World War
II---[is that **[destruction accomplishes little.]{.mark}**]{.underline}
[**[Adversaries]{.underline}**]{.mark} [typically [find **alternative
modes of survival**]{.mark}, [to
include **[innovative ](https://books.google.com/books?id=uyVivgAACAAJ&dq=vietcong+innovation&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwi82qnb9q_eAhURw1kKHbcEAEwQ6AEIMDAB)ways
of fighting on**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [much as Ho Chi Minh's army did
during the Vietnam War]{.underline}. Instead, [[warfare remains grounded
in the]{.mark} **time-honored [fundamentals]{.mark}**]{.underline}
[[of]{.underline} **[seizing territory]{.underline}** [and]{.underline}
**[exerting control]{.underline}**]{.mark} **[over
populations]{.underline}**. Such an observation, that war maintains this
historical consistency, was made most cogently by T. R. Fehrenbach in
1963, when he
astutely [recognized](https://books.google.com/books?id=OJNxAAAAMAAJ&dq=But%2C+pushbutton+warfare+meant+Armageddon%2C+and+Armageddon%2C+hopefully%2C+will+never+be+an+end+of+national+policy.+Americans+in+1950+rediscovered+something+that+since+Hiroshima+they+had+forgotten%3A+you+may+fly+over+a+land+forever%3B+you+may+bomb+it%2C+atomize+it%2C+pulverize+it+and+wipe+it+clean+of+life%E2%80%94but+if+you+desire+to+defend+it%2C+protect+it+and+keep+it+for+civilization%2C+you+must+do+this+on+the+ground%2C+the+way+the+Roman+legions+did%2C+by+putting+your+young+men+in+the+mud.&focus=searchwithinvolume&q=Americans+in+1950+rediscovered+something+that+since+Hiroshima+they+had+forgotten%3A+you+may+fly+over+a+land+forever%3B+you+may+bomb+it%2C+atomize+it%2C+pulverize+it+and+wipe+it+clean+of+life%E2%80%94but+if+you+desire+to+defend+it%2C+protect+it+and+keep+it+for+civilization%2C+you+must+do+this+on+the+ground%2C+the+way+the+Roman+legions+did%2C+by+putting+your+young+men+in+the+mud) a
fact of the US experience in the Korean War:

Americans in 1950 rediscovered something that since Hiroshima they had
forgotten: [[you may fly over a land forever]{.mark};]{.underline} [you
may bomb it]{.underline}, [atomize it, pulverize it and wipe it clean of
life]{.underline}---**[[but if you desire to defend
it]{.underline}]{.mark}**, **[protect it]{.underline}**
[and]{.underline} **[keep it]{.underline}** for civilization, [[you must
do this on the **ground**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [the way the **Roman
legions did**]{.underline}, by putting your young men in the mud.

Such an airpower-centric mindset increasingly permeates American
strategy toward Afghanistan, as the US military struggles to maintain
control. In October 2017, Secretary of Defense Jim Mattis loosened the
rules of engagement in Afghanistan, to
include [removing ](https://www.militarytimes.com/flashpoints/2017/10/03/mattis-reveals-new-rules-of-engagement/)"restrictions
that did not allow \[the American military\] to employ the air power
fully." Unfortunately, such firepower-intensive rules of engagement have
not improved the security situation in Afghanistan. In fact, as of
September 2018, the Taliban now control or contest [about 60 percent of
the
districts](https://www.longwarjournal.org/archives/2018/09/mapping-taliban-controlled-and-contested-districts-in-afghanistan-lwj-vs-us-military-assessments.php) in
Afghanistan, the largest [swath of
territory](https://www.longwarjournal.org/mapping-taliban-control-in-afghanistan) that
the Taliban have controlled since 2001. Taliban control will likely grow
even larger given
the [assassination ](https://foreignpolicy.com/2018/10/20/taliban-won-key-battle-afghanistan-future-election-abdul-raziq/)of
Kandahar Police Chief Abdul Raziq, a strongman client that had been able
to provide some modicum of security on behalf of the United States. This
should leave us wondering: [[What is the future of]{.mark} American
military power and [effectiveness in dealing with]{.mark} not only
**fragile states** such as Afghanistan]{.underline}, [but also
**[conventional threats emanating from]{.mark} rising, [near-pear
states?]{.mark}**]{.underline} This leaves us with the answer that
[[airpower is an **oversold panacea**]{.underline}]{.mark},
[and]{.underline} [[**[landpower]{.underline}** ]{.mark}](http://www.dtic.mil/dtic/tr/fulltext/u2/a594671.pdf)(i.e.,
substantial ground forces) combined with a viable strategy [[is the
**only plausible way of**]{.mark} **[achieving]{.mark} US national
security [objectives]{.mark}.**]{.underline}

Operation Allied Force and the Gulf War Hangover

Operation Allied Force (OAF) is traditionally used by airpower
enthusiasts as the prime example of airpower deciding a conflict
independent of the other armed services. For many airpower planners,
the [effects-based
approach](http://www.dtic.mil/dtic/tr/fulltext/u2/a515374.pdf) used to
decimate Iraqi centers of gravity in the Persian Gulf War justified a
similar method in trying to coerce and punish the Serbian government
for [atrocities ](https://books.google.com/books?id=p0gVDAAAQBAJ&printsec=frontcover&dq=Serb+atrocities+in+Kosovo&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwjetrqmzLPeAhXxtlkKHb7kAX0Q6AEIRzAF#v=onepage&q=Serb%20atrocities%20in%20Kosovo&f=false)in
Kosovo. However, such **[weak induction]{.underline}** [and]{.underline}
**[hasty generalizations]{.underline}** [by airpower enthusiasts
raise]{.underline} two [questions which they must answer, yet seldom
do.]{.underline}

[First]{.underline}, [if airpower won OAF alone, why did it take **so
long** to achieve a decisive victory?]{.underline} The typical response
to this question is that NATO-imposed restrictions limited the
effectiveness of the air campaign compared to the unleashed force that
characterized Desert Storm. The more logical argument is that [airpower
took so long]{.underline}, not due to self-imposed limitations, but
[because the **developed Serbian economy**]{.underline}, **[rough
terrain]{.underline}**, [and]{.underline} **[foliage]{.underline}**,
[simply **limited airpower's lethality**]{.underline}. Serbian President
Slobodan
[Milošević [believed ](https://books.google.com/books?id=fu1QiZ1n5AgC&pg=PA335&dq=Milosevic+hunkered+down&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwiphK3BoLHeAhVirlkKHeulDLEQ6AEIKDAA#v=onepage&q=Milosevic%20hunkered%20down&f=false)that
if he hunkered down, he could simply **outlast the
barrage.**]{.underline}

Next, [if the psychological implications of airpower can induce
capitulation, why did the slaughter of ethnic
Albanians [continue](https://books.google.com/books/about/The_Iraq_Wars_and_America_s_Military_Rev.html?id=0J3q-vpyrDkC&printsec=frontcover&source=kp_read_button#v=onepage&q=mcpeak's%20warning&f=false)]{.underline}---with
the genocidal rate actually increasing---[for seventy-eight days after
the initial strikes took place?]{.underline} In this case,
**[US]{.underline}** [and]{.underline} **[NATO bombings]{.underline}**
[only]{.underline} **[strengthened Milošević's resolve]{.underline}**
[and that of his **army**.]{.underline}

Interpretations of OAF and the conflict's resolution reveal a
fascinating dichotomy, which largely predicts how one views airpower
more generally. Traditionalists would say that airpower forced
Milošević's hand; yet the threat dynamics of a US/NATO [ground
invasion](https://books.google.com/books?id=0J3q-vpyrDkC&pg=PA125&dq=Milosevic+nato+ground+invasion+threat&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwic6sjtoLHeAhUCx1kKHV8ZBpoQ6AEINDAC#v=onepage&q=Milosevic%20nato%20ground%20invasion%20threat&f=false) carried
the day and finally forced him (and the Serbian army) to capitulate and
withdraw. Much like [Saddam
Hussein](https://books.google.com/books?id=Klzc6NpfKCMC&dq=saddam+miscalculated+persian+gulf+war&focus=searchwithinvolume&q=glaspie) during
the Persian Gulf War, Milošević made
political [calculations ](https://books.google.com/books?id=MyJKGq26ZjAC&printsec=frontcover&dq=Serbian+government+for+atrocities+in+Kosovo&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwjGmP3Ey7PeAhUiwFkKHYgQBMoQ6AEIQzAE#v=onepage&q=leverage&f=false)about
the resolve of the United States (and her allies) to commit to the
riskier use of ground forces (i.e., landpower).

[The Kosovo conflict]{.underline} provided many airpower traditionalists
the fodder needed to push airpower narratives of an offensive capability
that would transform warfare toward a future absent of land occupation.
However, the conflict instead [**illustrates the self-defeating
strategic intent of [airpower]{.mark}**. Its "**[effects]{.mark}**" [are
**so successful** and **low-risk that**, **by its very nature**, it
leads to a **seductive illusion**]{.mark}]{.underline}
[[that]{.underline} **[landpower is not
necessary]{.underline}**]{.mark}. [[That]{.mark} illusion]{.underline},
however, **[[fails to comprehend]{.mark} the dynamic nature of
[adversarial decision making]{.mark}]{.underline}** [and ability to
easily **adapt** to **airpower-centric coercion**]{.underline}. [Since
landpower requires [**engagement** and
**commitment**](https://mwi.usma.edu/201425simplifying-strategic-concepts-thinking-about-what-landpower-seapower-airpower-and-the-marines-ought-to-do/)]{.underline},
[political and military leadership need to create the
**necessary **[**willpower** ](https://thestrategybridge.org/the-bridge/2017/11/5/the-death-of-american-conventional-warfare-its-the-political-willpower-stupid)to
support landpower objectives]{.underline}. Such purposes can range from
attrition to establishing territorial control, all of which should
support strategic level goals.

Airpower Enables, but Landpower with a Strategy is Vital

Strategic effectiveness on the modern battlefield in the twenty-first
century is centered on the reality that
airpower needs [landpower ](https://mwi.usma.edu/2014512defining-and-conceptualizing-strategic-landpower/)to
be strategically relevant. Landpower, by contrast, merely desires
airpower because it makes both offensive and defensive maneuvers less
risky by degrading and disrupting adversarial ground forces. There is a
co-dependence between the two, but it is unequal. That fact is perhaps
threatening to some advocates of airpower, but it need not be.
Airpower's decisiveness might be in question---domination in the air
domain does not equal domination in the ground domain---but
its relevance is unequivocally not. Both the kinetic effects and
non-kinetic capabilities afforded by airpower can favorably shape
battlefield outcomes for landpower.

[[Against **near-peer state adversaries**]{.underline}, [airpower
provides]{.underline}]{.mark} [tremendous [kinetic
power]{.mark}]{.underline} against fixed targets.
[**[However]{.underline}**, [due to]{.underline}]{.mark} [contemporary
understandings of **[international law]{.mark}**]{.underline} (and what
are legal targets), [[adversaries **rarely allow** the United States to
**target critical assets**]{.underline}]{.mark}---[and the United States
and her allies generally **refrain from purposely inflicting collateral
damage**]{.underline}. For instance, [during the Vietnam
War]{.underline}, [the State Department regularly informed North
Vietnamese leadership what targets were going to be bombed by the US
military so as to avoid civilian casualties]{.underline}. **[[The most
airpower can provide]{.underline}]{.mark}** [in the sphere of military
operations against adversarial states [is]{.mark} primarily
**[non-kinetic effects]{.mark}**]{.underline}---things like ISR
(intelligence, surveillance, and reconnaissance) and C2 (command and
control) capabilities. **[Moreover]{.underline}**, [with
the [development ](https://www.mitpressjournals.org/doi/pdf/10.1162/ISEC_a_00249)of
**A2/AD**]{.underline} (antiaccess and area denial) [weapon systems by
potentially **adversarial states**]{.underline} [to **detect stealth
aircraft** and launch accurate, long-range
surface-to-air-missiles]{.underline}, [the prospect of American airpower
achieving **nothing more than air parity**]{.underline} [in certain
regions is close to being a **reality**.]{.underline}

In regard to non-state actors, **[ground forces]{.underline}** [still
have to deal with the
same [dynamics ](https://books.google.com/books/about/Civil_Wars.html?id=C8wCDgAAQBAJ&printsec=frontcover&source=kp_read_button#v=onepage&q=widespread%20and%20destrutive&f=false)seen
in civil wars since the [First Jewish--Roman
War](https://books.google.com/books?id=x9dRDwAAQBAJ&printsec=frontcover&dq=josephus+first+jewish+roman+war&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwie7KGWxKneAhWMslkKHYQgA74Q6AEIMTAC#v=onepage&q=josephus%20first%20jewish%20roman%20war&f=false)]{.underline},
nearly two millennia ago. [[There are local-level politics that **no
amount of airpower effects**]{.underline}]{.mark}, kinetic or
non-kinetic, [[can **supplant** or **overcome**]{.underline}]{.mark}.
[There might be competition between pro-government soldiers and
anti-government fighters]{.underline} taking place seemingly on every
street corner and in every neighborhood---[and this is the dynamic
environment in which both sides are vying for control while the local
population tries to hedge its bets]{.underline}, simply seeking
survival. In this precarious situation, [the armed force that
eventually [gains
control](https://books.google.com/books?id=jaTUJZlOykkC&printsec=frontcover&dq=kalyvas+logic&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwiOg4S44areAhXs64MKHZNeAGQQuwUIKzAA#v=snippet&q=information%20violence&f=false) does
so because of its ability to **better collect
intelligence**]{.underline} (especially through collaborators and
defectors) [and **discriminately** kill adversarial forces]{.underline}
(and their supporters and sympathizers). Even attempts to use
technology, such as mechanized formation or armored vehicles to protect
counterinsurgent forces in such a situation, [reduces the
ability](https://www.cambridge.org/core/services/aop-cambridge-core/content/view/7D087F93A59E7D15931576039C0641BF/S0020818309090031a.pdf/rage-against-the-machines-explaining-outcomes-in-counterinsurgency-wars.pdf) of
counterinsurgents to collect accurate, detailed, and nuanced
intelligence, thereby degrading their ability to selectively target and
fight insurgents among the population.

The fact that there has been so much American military investment in and
emphasis
on [building ](https://www.the-american-interest.com/2017/02/07/training-wreck/)the
Afghan Air Force with little to show in terms of Afghan combat
effectiveness against the Taliban is not an indictment of the Afghan
military forces. It is a reflection of a strategic delusion, an
illustration of what the United States values in the war effort in
Afghanistan---and what the Afghan military is actually capable of. The
first [deployment ](https://www.stripes.com/news/with-1st-sfab-deployed-army-looks-to-build-more-adviser-brigades-1.519734)of
a security force assistance brigade to Afghanistan in early 2018---with
the specific intent to develop an effective Afghan National
Army---might help the war American war effort to overcome the [three
common
traps](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/326534224_Getting_American_Security_Force_Assistance_Right_Political_Context_Matters_Forthcoming_in_Joint_Forces_Quarterly) of
security force assistance missions (politicized, unaffordable, and
threatening) in fragile states. There is a moral hazard involved with
training and supporting host-nation armies in weak states, because this
can lead to a dependency on American firepower without resolving
domestic political issues and civil-military relations problems that
prevent the host-nation military from being effective and sustainable in
the long term. Thus, airpower and landpower need to be further
re-conceptualized around the idea of how American military power can be
utilized in these two domains. Understanding which explicit effects can
be obtained through each is vital in supporting long-term strategic
interests, such as nation-and-state building in fragile states (e.g.,
Afghanistan, Iraq, Somalia, etc.). Otherwise even air- and landpower
effects together become, at best, part of attritional wars that amount
to nothing more than a game
of [whack-a-mole](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/09636412.2014.935229).

Conclusion: Airpower Needs to Enable Ground Forces

Airpower is inherently an enabling force. Airpower advocates have long
argued that it can generate strategic effects absent landpower. As with
many contentions, this is correct if the context is set tightly. More
importantly, non-kinetic airpower (e.g., intelligence and signals
collection) provides tremendous value to all military branches and
allies seeking to target important nodes in criminal, insurgent, or
terrorist networks and also against adversarial states.

OAF represents the most recent example of airpower failing to achieve
the desired effects of capitulation from an adversarial state with a
developed infrastructure and economy. Only under the perceived threat of
a ground invasion did Milošević finally conclude that his efforts would
fail. Ultimately, there is an indisputable feature of [survival
concerns](https://books.google.com/books?id=TjmpCQAAQBAJ&printsec=frontcover&dq=phil+haun+air+power+coercion&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwi60rGKz7PeAhXrx1kKHUBLDhUQ6AEIKjAA#v=onepage&q=survival%20concerns&f=false) seen
in most weak state contexts, where coercion throughout all the domains
is the surest way to achieve strategic goals.

There is inherent virtue in advocating for greater "jointness" between
all branches in all the different domains, especially given the current
strategic context---the United States having been sobered by the
elusiveness of clear-cut victories in Iraq and Afghanistan and the
post--Cold War "unipolar moment" seemingly over as the United States
faces a revanchist Russia and the continued rise of China. But separate
from this, there is a simple truth: airpower ultimately needs landpower
to remain strategically important. Landpower can operate without
airpower, if ground commanders can accept a higher level of risk.
Airpower without landpower or strategic purpose is just [bombing to
win](https://books.google.com/books?hl=en&lr=&id=xHZnTAPQFc8C&oi=fnd&pg=PR7&dq=bombing+to+win&ots=IKZZADoIiZ&sig=OWsJoSYfbNgGxEDNSNnIc69kwhs#v=onepage&q=bombing%20to%20win&f=false), without
actually winning.

Luckily, American and allied ground forces have been able to
operate---for decades---under the presumption of the air domain being
dominated by friendly forces achieving and maintaining air supremacy.
The more complicated aspect of this relationship has been trying to
convert tactical victories in the short term into successes at the
strategic level in the long term. [Perhaps]{.underline}
**[American [[over-reliance on
airpower]{.mark}](https://www.theatlantic.com/international/archive/2016/03/obama-doctrine-goldberg-realist-isolationist/473205/)]{.underline}**---[by]{.underline}
**[political leaders]{.underline}**---[[is]{.mark} precisely [why the US
military **struggles** to **achieve**]{.mark} **national security
[objectives abroad]{.mark}**]{.underline}. As Dr. John
Farquhar [suggests](https://www.airuniversity.af.edu/Portals/10/ASPJ/journals/Volume-31_Issue-1/V-Farquhar.pdf),
"Airpower has to be used within a comprehensive political strategy;
**[airpower alone]{.underline}**, especially kinetic air strikes,
**[cannot]{.underline}** [substitute for sound policy]{.underline}."

While there might be some merit to [discussions about relying on
[airpower]{.mark} to achieve certain effects]{.underline} to defeat
adversaries, such as the Islamic State, this [**[short-circuits the
primary center of gravity]{.underline}** [in]{.underline}]{.mark} [most
[underdeveloped countries: **state formation**]{.mark}]{.underline}. [If
one takes a **long view of history**]{.underline}, [then it might have
been **strategically wise**]{.underline}---in terms of state-building
objectives---[that the US military reluctantly engaged in a **minimalist
fashion**]{.underline} (i.e., with advisers, fire support, air support,
etc.) [in supporting the Iraqi Army against the Islamic
State]{.underline}. The reluctant American approach forced politicians
in Baghdad to move beyond their corrupt survival strategies (e.g.,
patronage, coup-proofing, etc.), thereby allowing Iraqi military forces
to operate with maximum combat effectiveness. Thus, numerous domestic
political issues that had fueled Sunni grievances, which begat Islamic
State momentum in the first place, were resolved by the existential
threat [posed ](https://www.cbsnews.com/news/isis-within-8-miles-of-baghdad-airport-and-armed-with-manpads/)by
Islamic State fighters getting within eight miles of the Baghdad Airport
in October 2014.

[[Airpower]{.underline} **[cannot resolve]{.underline}**]{.mark} **[the
[political dynamics]{.mark}]{.underline}** [[that]{.underline}
**[distort state building]{.underline}**, [but]{.underline}]{.mark} [the
**[presence of ground troops can]{.mark} serve as a third-party
enforcement mechanism**]{.underline} [when political relations **break
down** between various factions.]{.underline} Much like the NATO-led
peacekeeping force in Kosovo has maintained a sizeable presence of
troops in Kosovo [since
1999](https://www.army.mil/article/201778/jmrc_prepares_units_for_kosovo_mission),
perhaps American policymakers need to accept a similar strategy in Iraq.
Deploying a similar type of US-led international peacekeeping force in
Iraq might be needed for several decades so as to prevent future Iraqi
political factionalism and military fragmentation. A long-term
engagement could institutionalize trust between various factions in the
Iraqi government and security organizations, allowing the US military to
eventually pull out without worrying about the Iraqi government or
military collapsing.

Thus, [[while many will keep arguing]{.mark} for the **unleashing of
[airpower]{.mark}** to maximize combat effectiveness]{.underline},
**[[we should be wary]{.mark} of such suggestions]{.underline}**. [[We
need to ask **tougher**]{.mark} **[questions]{.mark}**
[about]{.mark}]{.underline} how airpower will support [**[landpower
efforts]{.underline}** [to]{.underline}
**[stabilize]{.underline}**]{.mark} **[and secure [weak
states]{.mark}]{.underline}** [[throughout]{.underline} **[Asia, Africa,
and the Middle East]{.underline}**]{.mark}. [[Answering]{.mark} **these
questions** [will be **central** to whether the United States **wins or
fails**]{.mark} in these efforts]{.underline}. Regardless, **[landpower
will remain a critical ingredient in American strategic
success]{.underline}**.

### Airpower Bad\-\--Failed States\-\--Impact\-\--2NC

#### Outweighs on [magnitude]{.underline}.

Richard W. **Rahn 15**, senior fellow at Cato, Chairman of the Institute
for Global Economic Growth, and serves on the editorial board of the
Cayman Financial Review, "The Rise of the Failed States",
https://www.cato.org/commentary/rise-failed-states

[[The]{.underline} **[greatest threat to global security]{.underline}**
[is]{.underline}]{.mark} the rapidly increasing number of **[[failed
states]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Even though there is no agreed‐​upon
definition of a failed state, [it is generally understood that when
a government can no longer provide basic security to its people due to
a rise in violence or extreme poverty]{.underline}, or loses control
over part of its territory to domestic or foreign terrorist groups, [the
state has failed.]{.underline}

#### Draws in [great powers]{.underline}.

Jakub **Grygiel 9**, PhD, Professor of Politics at the Catholic
University of America and fellow at The Institute for Human Ecology,
"Vacuum Wars",
https://www.the-american-interest.com/2009/07/01/vacuum-wars/

[Mention "failed states" in an academic seminar]{.underline} or a policy
meeting [and you will hear a laundry list of tragic
problems]{.underline}: poverty, disease, famine, refugees flowing across
borders and more. If it is a really gloomy day, you will hear that
failed states are associated with terrorism, ethnic cleansing and
genocide. This is the conventional wisdom that has developed over the
past two decades, and rightly so given the scale of the human tragedies
in Bosnia, Somalia and Rwanda, just to mention the most egregious cases
of the 1990s.

[This]{.underline} **[prevailing view]{.underline}** [of failed states,
however, though true, is also **incomplete**]{.underline}. [[Failed
states are]{.mark} not only a source of **domestic**
calamities]{.underline}; [they are also [potentially a source of **great
power competition**]{.mark}]{.underline} [[that]{.mark} in the past
[has]{.mark} often [led to **confrontation, crisis and
war**]{.mark}]{.underline}. [The]{.underline} **[[failure of a
state]{.underline}]{.mark}** [[creates a **vacuum**]{.underline}
[that]{.underline}]{.mark}[, especially [in **strategically important
regions**]{.mark}]{.underline}[, [draws in]{.underline}]{.mark}
**[competitive [great-power intervention]{.mark}]{.underline}**. This
more traditional view of state failure is less prevalent these days, for
only recently has the prospect of great power competition over failed
"vacuum" states returned. But, clearly, recent events in Georgia---as
well as possible future scenarios in Iraq, Afghanistan and Pakistan, as
well as southeastern Europe, Asia and parts of Africa---suggest that it
might be a good time to adjust, really to expand, the way we think about
"failed states" and the kinds of problems they can cause.

### Airpower Bad\-\--Failed States\-\--Impact\-\--AT: Defense\-\--2NC

#### Impact defense is wrong\-\--failed states are the [greatest]{.underline} existential threat.

Lester R. **Brown 9,** Founder of both the WorldWatch Institute and the
Earth Policy Institute, Scientific American, "Could Food Shortages Bring
Down Civilization?" Ebsco

**[[The Problem of Failed States]{.mark}]{.underline}**

Even a cursory look at the vital signs of our current world order lends
unwelcome support to my conclusion. And those of us in the environmental
field are well into our third decade of charting trends of environmental
decline without seeing any significant effort to reverse a single one.

In six of the past nine years world grain production has fallen short of
consumption, forcing a steady drawdown in stocks. When the 2008 harvest
began, world carryover stocks of grain (the amount in the bin when the
new harvest begins) were at 62 days of consumption, a near record low.
In response, world grain prices in the spring and summer of last year
climbed to the highest level ever.

As demand for food rises faster than supplies are growing, the resulting
food-price inflation puts severe stress on the governments of countries
already teetering on the edge of chaos. Unable to buy grain or grow
their own, hungry people take to the streets. Indeed, even before the
steep climb in grain prices in 2008, the number of failing states was
expanding \[see sidebar at left\]. Many of their problem\'s stem from a
failure to slow the growth of their populations. But if the food
situation continues to deteriorate, **[[entire nations will break down
at an ever increasing rate]{.underline}]{.mark}**. We have entered a new
era in geopolitics. **[[In the 20th century the main threat]{.mark} to
international security [was superpower conflict; today it is failing
states.]{.mark}]{.underline}** It is not the concentration of power but
its absence that puts us at risk.

[States fail when national governments can no longer
provide]{.underline} [personal security, food security and basic social
services]{.underline} such as education and health care.
[They]{.underline} often [lose control of part or all of their
territory.]{.underline} [When governments lose their monopoly on power,
**law and order begin to disintegrate**]{.underline}. After a point,
countries can become so dangerous that food relief workers are no longer
safe and their programs are halted; in Somalia and Afghanistan,
deteriorating conditions have already put such programs in jeopardy.

[[Failing states **are of international concern**]{.mark} because [they
are a source of **terrorists**, **drugs**, **weapons** and **refugees**,
threatening **political stability everywhere**]{.mark}.]{.underline}
Somalia, number one on the 2008 list of failing states, has become a
base for piracy. Iraq, number five, is a hotbed for terrorist training.
Afghanistan, number seven, is the world\'s leading supplier of heroin.
Following the massive genocide of 1994 in Rwanda, refugees from that
troubled state, thousands of armed soldiers among them, helped to
destabilize neighboring Democratic Republic of the Congo (number six).

[Our [**global civilization** depends on a **functioning network
of**]{.mark} **politically [healthy nation-states]{.mark}** [to
control]{.mark} the spread of infectious **[disease]{.mark}**, to manage
the international monetary system, to control international
[**terrorism** and]{.mark} to reach [**scores of other common goals**.
If the system]{.mark} for controlling infectious
diseases]{.underline}\--such as polio, SARS or avian flu\--[**[breaks
down, humanity will be in trouble]{.mark}**. Once states fail, no one
assumes responsibility for their debt to outside lenders. If enough
states disintegrate, [**their fall will threaten the stability of global
civilization itself**.]{.mark}]{.underline}

### Airpower Bad\-\--Failed States\-\--Impact\-\--AT: No Scenario

#### Global crises\-\--and corresponding US [engagement]{.underline} in [hotspots]{.underline}\-\-\--is [inevitable]{.underline}. It's just a question of [what strategy]{.underline} we employ.

Daniel **Immerwahr 20**, PhD, professor of history at Northwestern,
specializing in twentieth-century U.S. history within a global context,
"Should America Still Police the World?",
https://www.newyorker.com/books/under-review/should-america-still-police-the-world

**[Such is the way of hegemony]{.underline}**, argues Porter.
Rule-makers aren't generally rule-takers. Porter, a realist in the
classical tradition, is untroubled by this. What bothers him is the
"false promise" that hegemony can be benevolent---a delusion, he
believes, that warps the world view of [U.S. leaders]{.underline}. From
their perspective, the international order created in 1945 has been a
blessing. They [have]{.underline} thus [struggled to understand why
foreigners might not be content or might perceive U.S. policing as a
threat]{.underline}. Puffed up on their own hype, policymakers in
Washington have been repeatedly "shocked" when their "obviously benign
actions result in resistance," Porter writes. Seeing themselves as pure,
they can explain opposition only by seeing the world as wicked: a
sinkhole teeming with thugs, bad guys, and ever-duplicitous Pakistanis.
"You know why they hate us?" George W. Bush asked after Al Qaeda's
attacks on September 11, 2001. "They hate us because we love freedom."

[Such a world view **leads easily**]{.underline} [to the conclusion that
**[Something Must Be Done]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [Bush started Middle
Eastern wars in the name of freedom]{.underline}---[wars
that]{.underline}, in different forms, **[Obama]{.underline}**
[and]{.underline} **[Trump continued]{.underline}**. The titles of the
military missions read like tombstones in a graveyard of liberal
ambitions: [Operation Iraqi Freedom, Operation Enduring Freedom,
Operation Freedom's Sentinel, Operation New Dawn. We're now at Operation
Inherent Resolve]{.underline}, the ongoing mission to extirpate the
Islamic State in Iraq and Syria. It's the first one whose name makes no
reference to freedom or transformation, yet [it would be **optimistic**
to **interpret this as a backing down**]{.underline}. [[Recent years
have shown **Washington's military commitments** to be **wedged
tightly**]{.mark} **[in place]{.mark}**]{.underline}. This past
September, [on the same day that Trump declared "we are out of Syria"
other than to guard its oil fields, the Pentagon announced that it was
increasing troop levels by twenty per cent.]{.underline}

"[[You guys want me to **send troops everywhere**]{.underline}]{.mark},"
[[Trump]{.mark} reportedly [complained]{.mark}]{.underline} to his top
national-security aides as they proposed more force in North Africa.
"**[[Unfortunately, sir]{.underline}]{.mark}**," [Secretary of Defense
Jim [Mattis replied]{.mark}]{.underline}, "**[[you have no
choice]{.underline}]{.mark}**."

[Does the United States, in the end, have a choice?]{.underline} **[Not
really]{.underline}**, writes Gates. With U.S. primacy, the world is a
place where differences can be resolved peaceably. [Without it, the
planet "descends into a **dog-eat-dog**]{.underline},
**[might-makes-right]{.underline}** [environment]{.underline}." **[[The
question]{.underline}]{.mark}** for Gates [[is **not whether the United
States should reign but how it should do so**]{.mark}**.**]{.underline}
A defter hand, he hopes, will restore the country to its necessary role.
This restoration would, as Biden has put it, "place America back at the
head of the table."

Porter has different advice: abandon the seat. The U.S.-centered system
was built at the end of the Second World War, when the U.S. economy was
larger than the four next-largest economies combined. Today, by some
measures, it is smaller than China's. The crisis of U.S. supremacy has
been in the making for decades, though Porter thinks that it has now
reached its culmination. For the United States to hang onto its
increasingly untenable position would only leave it "a militarized,
overextended empire in a permanent state of alarm," perpetually
threatening the peace it claims to protect. A better course for the
obsolescent hegemon, Porter proposes, would be to "decline gracefully."

What might a [graceful decline]{.underline} look like? It [would require
Washington to give up the notion that its troops or its values should
dominate the planet]{.underline}. This wouldn't mean retreating from
worldly affairs, just adjusting to a more level international field.
Relieved of having to frantically defend its leadership, Porter notes,
the United States could focus, as it had before the Second World War, on
the more achievable goals of protecting itself from physical attack and
defending its republican institutions.

Both tasks would be easier with a smaller military. It's hard to be a
calm and secure republic with armed forces stationed everywhere,
stirring up resistance. A dethroned Washington would still need to
maneuver against powerful rivals---Porter recommends that the United
States contain China and block it from forming a strong alliance with
Russia. Yet it would no longer have to compulsively monitor the map for
threats to its primacy.

**[[Relinquishing]{.mark} that [primacy]{.mark}]{.underline}**---[giving
up the seat at the head of the table]{.underline}---**[[is about as
palatable]{.underline}]{.mark}** [to U.S. policymakers [as **defunding
police departments**]{.mark}. It's not just that the position is
**unpopular**]{.underline} among them; [it's that]{.underline}, until
recently, [it was]{.underline} in many circles
**[unthinkable]{.underline}**. But is that because the idea is so
horrendously bad that it would quickly trigger global catastrophe, as
Gates believes? Or is U.S. hegemony yet another relic from a bygone era,
long overdue for reappraisal?

## AT: Alaskan Economy ADV

### Economy\-\--SMRs Worse\-\--1NC

#### SMR's are [bad for the Economy]{.underline} and [too slow to solve climate change]{.underline}. UAMPS's failed project proves. 

M.V. **Ramana '24**\-\--M.V. Ramana is the Simons Chair in Disarmament,
Global and Human Security and Professor at the School of Public Policy
and Global Affairs, at the University of British Columbia in Vancouver,
Canada. ("The collapse of NuScale's project should spell the end for
small modular nuclear reactors", Utility Dive,
https://www.utilitydive.com/news/nuscale-uamps-project-small-modular-reactor-ramanasmr-/705717/,
1-31-2024). Accessed 7-6-2025 by NT-RM.

[This past November]{.underline}, the Utah Associated Municipal Power
Systems, or [**UAMPS, terminated** what was to be "the **first NuScale
Power s**mall **m**odular **r**eactor plant to begin operation in the
United States]{.underline}." [This was a **death
foretold**]{.underline}; [the **red flags** have been obvious for
years]{.underline} now. Although there were problems specific to that
project, [the **financial challenges** and **cost trends**
witnessed]{.underline} in this case [will afflict any small modular
nuclear reactor project. In a rational world, **no utility or government
would invest another dime** on these theoretical reactor
concepts.]{.underline}

As announced in 2015[, the UAMPS project initially involved constructing
12 reactor modules capable of generating 600 MW, with the aim
of]{.underline} starting operations "around 2023," and at [an "overnight
cost" of \$3 billion. In 2018, NuScale announced a design modification
with each module now producing 60 MW of electricity,]{.underline} or 720
MW for the whole plant, claiming this would lower the cost "on a per
kilowatt basis from an expected \$5,000 to approximately \$4,200."

[The estimated costs of the project **rose to \$4.2 billion** in 2018,
**then \$6.1 billion** in 2020, and **finally \$9.3 billion** in
2023,]{.underline} after it was scaled down to 462 MW in 2021. [In the
end, the **costs were clearly too high** for UAMPS members to
bear]{.underline}.

[The engineers and accountants on the project were **evidently
unrealistic** about the likely costs]{.underline}, or perhaps NuScale
preferred to gently break the bad news about how immense the bill would
be. Or both. NuScale also managed to retain members by claiming an
unreasonably low cost of power from the project once operational, a cost
derived using an opaque economic methodology without clarity over its
assumptions. [There is a lesson here: one just **cannot trust initial
cost estimates for nuclear reactors** and their
electricity]{.underline}.

[The UAMPS project is no exception, and just **adds one more data
point** to a **long history of cost** and **time overruns** for nuclear
power projects]{.underline}. [A]{.underline} 2014 [academic study
examined 180 nuclear power projects around the world and found **175 of
them exceeded the initial budget** by an average of **117%** by the time
they were completed. They also took]{.underline}, on average**[, 64%
longer than projected.]{.underline}**

[More recent projects have **fared worse**]{.underline}. For example,
the only reactor being constructed in France --- the poster child for
nuclear energy --- is Flamanville 3 with an estimated cost of 13.2
billion euros (around \$15 billion) --- four times the forecast when
construction started. The time anticipated has gone from 4.5 years
initially to over 16 years.

[These **high costs translate to expensive electricity**. In April
2023]{.underline}, Lazard, [a financial firm, estimated that the
unsubsidized levelized cost of electricity from new nuclear plants in
the U.S. will be]{.underline} between \$141 and [**\$221** per megawatt
hour. By comparison, a newly constructed **utility-scale solar
facility** with some storage to provide power after the sun sets will
produce power at an unsubsidized levelized cost of]{.underline} between
**[\$46]{.underline}** and \$102 [per megawatt hour,]{.underline}
according to Lazard. [Costs for these technologies have been trending in
opposite directions: **nuclear is going up** whereas **solar** and
**batteries have become cheaper** and are expected to decline
further.]{.underline}

[Small modular reactors are at an **economic
disadvantage**]{.underline}. [The lower power output of these reactors,
**less than 300 MW per unit**]{.underline} by definition as compared to
the roughly 1,000 MW for the typical reactors that have been constructed
for over four decades, [means **less revenue** for the owning utility.
But the **cost of construction** is not proportionately smaller.
Engineers call this economies of scale. In terms of cost per unit
(megawatt) of generation capacity, SMRs and the electricity they produce
will be **more expensive**]{.underline} than power from large nuclear
plants currently under construction. As the Lazard estimates show, these
large [plants are]{.underline} themselves **[not competitive with
renewables.]{.underline}**

The [lack of "economies of scale" is apparent for the UAMPS
project]{.underline}. Its [final cost was]{.underline} around [**250%
more** than the initial per megawatt cost for the 2,200 MW Vogtle
project in Georgia]{.underline}. But [the phenomenon of **higher costs
for s**mall **r**eactors is general, and will **plague all SMR
proposals**]{.underline}. On a per megawatt basis, the estimated cost of
the CAREM-25 SMR project being built in Argentina is estimated to be
between \$17 million to \$24 million, in the same ballpark as the UAMPS
project. [Historically]{.underline}, too, [most of the early small
reactors built in the United States **shut down** because of adverse
economics]{.underline}.

Nuclear energy advocates point to the variable nature of renewables and
argue that some form of nuclear power is necessary to keep the power
system reliable. [But grids have always had to deal with variable
supply, including from supposedly reliable sources of power: nuclear
plants have to be **shut down unexpectedly** for safety
reasons]{.underline} (for example, Japan's plants after the Fukushima
disaster) and coal plants might not operate because their fuel supply is
interrupted, for example, by the derailment of a train. [Every French
nuclear plant was, on average, shut down for **96.2 days**, **115.4
days**, **103.8 days**, and **152** days in 2019, 2020, 2021, and 2022,
respectively.]{.underline}

The variability of modern renewables can be addressed by a combination
of increasing diversity in technologies and in geographical locations,
providing incentives to consumers to shift their electricity use to
periods when renewable energy is plentiful, and finally expanding
investments into multiple forms of energy storage, from lithium-ion
batteries to pumped storage of water. But even prior to all that is
encouraging more efficient use of energy. This also helps address
variability because, for example, a building that gains or loses heat
more slowly can remain comfortable for longer periods of time, even
during periods of reduced electricity supply. [Nuclear plants are **not
needed** to compensate for the periods when the sun is not shining or
the wind is not blowing.]{.underline}

All of this also means that [nuclear power is not needed to solve the
climate crisis. Not only are nuclear reactors **very expensive**, they
also take a **very long time to build**. And this is **time we don't
have** because the climate crisis is **urgent**.]{.underline}

When announcing the end of the [UAMPS project, NuScale's Chief Executive
Officer said]{.underline} on a conference call: ["Once **you're on a
dead horse**, you dismount quickly. That's where we are here." The dead
horse, in reality, **is all the SMR projects**. The sooner we get off
this misguided quest, the more we can **focus on rationally dealing with
our energy and climate challenges**]{.underline}.

### Economy\-\--AT: EVs\-\--1NC

#### EVs are [inaccessible]{.underline}, [prohibitively expensive]{.underline}, and [emissions-intensive]{.underline}\-\--while some [are inevitable]{.underline}, only [using less]{.underline} can facilitate [net-zero]{.underline}. 

**Bennett and Hitt 23**, \*PhD, Policy Director of Life: Powered, an
initiative at the Texas Public Policy Foundation, \*\*Manager for the
Texas Public Policy Foundation (\*Brent Bennett, \*\*Andrea Hitt,
November 21, 2023, "The Biden Administration's EV Goals Are an Expensive
Fantasy," Real Clear Energy,
https://www.realclearenergy.org/articles/2023/11/21/the_biden_administrations_ev_goals_are_an_expensive_fantasy_994010.html)

[[For **gas**]{.mark}oline **vehicles**, [the price]{.mark} you see [at
the]{.mark} gas [**pump** covers]{.mark} the [cost of
**extracting**]{.mark}, refining, and transporting]{.underline} the
gasoline, [[but the same **cannot be said** for]{.underline}]{.mark} the
cost of charging [[an **EV**. EVs require]{.mark} **new
[charging]{.mark}**]{.underline} infrastructure, [[and]{.mark} their
large **power draw** increases the **[strain]{.mark}** on [electricity
**infrastructure**]{.mark}]{.underline}. As our research highlights, a
typical EV charging overnight at home consumes as much power as several
homes, and an EV charging at a fast-charging station in 30 minutes
consumes as much power as a small to medium-sized grocery store. A few
extra EVs in the neighborhoods are manageable, but widespread EV
adoption will require significant and expensive grid upgrades.

Adding insult to injury, EV owners alone aren't shouldering these
increased electricity costs, which average \$11,833 per vehicle over 10
years. Until a utility starts charging EV owners for the extra
infrastructure costs to serve them, those costs are shared among all the
utility's customers. Residential electricity costs across the U.S. have
risen 20% over the last three years, and a rapid forced adoption of EVs
will only make this problem worse.

Direct federal and state subsidies provide EVs with another \$8,984 per
vehicle over 10 years, including the widely publicized \$7,500 federal
tax credit in the so-called Inflation Reduction Act and smaller state
subsidies for EVs. All these subsidies, of course, are borne by the
American taxpayer.

President Biden's expensive green pipe dream is not without irony.

While Biden administration claims that these draconian EV mandates are
necessary to combat climate change, the widespread adoption of EVs in
the developed world would have negligible effects on global emissions
and climate. For starters, if EVs are able to displace all the carbon
emissions from U.S. passenger cars, that would only cut out 20% of U.S.
carbon emissions. Our calculations show that even if the U.S. eliminated
all of its carbon emissions by 2050, the effect on global temperatures
in 2100 would only be 0.08 degrees Celsius.

But EVs will not even get us that far because they don't cut carbon
emissions much---if at all---compared  to gasoline vehicles. As pointed
out by Mark Mills in a recent op-ed in Real Clear Energy, it is nearly
impossible to measure an individual EV's emissions. While driving an EV
itself does not directly produce emissions, the emissions to generate
the electricity used to charge EVs vary widely depending on location.

[[EV **batteries**]{.mark} also [require **fossil fuels** to
produce]{.mark}]{.underline}, and many components of EV batteries are
made in emissions-heavy China. The [[emissions]{.mark} resulting [from
**mining** and **processing**]{.mark} the materials]{.underline} used in
the battery are largely unreported, and the emissions during EV
production [[could]{.underline}]{.mark} potentially [be enough to
**[wipe out]{.mark}** the **[emissions saved]{.mark}** by not combusting
gasoline]{.underline}.

A recent study by Volvo attempts to quantify some of these factors, and
the result is not rosy for EVs. The lifetime emissions of the electric
version of the Volvo SUV at the center of the study are only a third
less than the emissions of the gasoline version, and that is when it is
charged on the carbon-light European grid. Different assumptions could
lead to an EV emitting more carbon than its gasoline counterpart. The
obvious conclusion is that without rapid reductions in carbon emissions
from the electric grid, an equally Herculean task to EV mass adoption,
EVs will continue to produce significant carbon emissions.

Emissions from gasoline vehicles are projected to decline 20% over the
next decade, and hybrids, which nearly double the fuel efficiency of a
gasoline vehicle with a battery that is 50-100 times smaller than an EV
battery, would actually produce the least amount of lifetime emissions.
But the net-zero advocates are needlessly demanding all EVs---or
nothing.

EVs would also have little impact on levels of actual pollution in U.S.
cities, like soot and smog, because the U.S. is already a world leader
in clean air. When the number of passenger cars on the road fell by half
during the height of the COVID-19 pandemic in 2020, there was no
measurable impact on air quality in the U.S. Our air pollution levels
are so close their natural state that weather has a far greater impact
on pollution levels in most U.S. cities than the emissions from our
vehicles.

[The [reality is, **EVs** are **not going away**]{.mark} any time soon,
[but **neither** are **cars**]{.mark} in general. [Americans
are]{.mark}]{.underline} still **[[driving]{.underline}]{.mark}**
at nearly [[the **same rate**]{.mark} they were
[before]{.mark}]{.underline} the **[[COVID]{.underline}]{.mark}**-19
pandemic---[more than [**3.2 trillion** miles]{.mark} total
**[annually]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Even the addition of a few hundred
million [**[new EVs]{.mark}** over [the next
decade]{.mark}]{.underline}, up from 20 million today, [[will
**only account**]{.mark} for]{.underline} approximately 10-[[**20%**
of]{.mark} all]{.underline} passenger [[vehicles
**globally**]{.underline}]{.mark}.\[GU1\] \[GU2\] \[3\]  Currently, 90%
of EVs in the U.S. are purchased as a second or third car, usually in
addition to a gasoline vehicle. If the U.S. were to adopt the
[Biden]{.underline} administration's preferred number [EVs,
[consumers]{.mark} would [have to **purchase** EVs]{.mark} at a
scale]{.underline} and velocity [[**10 times**
greater]{.underline}]{.mark} and faster [[than **any**]{.mark}
new]{.underline} model [[car **in history**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

[[Even **this isn't enough**]{.underline}]{.mark} to achieve the left's
dystopian net-zero goals. The International Energy
Agency forecasts \[GU4\] [the [**number** of]{.mark}]{.underline} global
[[households **without a car** needs to rise]{.underline}]{.mark} from
45% today [[to **70%** to achieve **net-zero**]{.mark} by 2050. That's
right---[70%]{.mark} of people]{.underline} around the world [[must
**not** have a **car**]{.underline}]{.mark} to meet the global elite's
climate goals. Most of [the]{.underline} 45% of [households]{.underline}
who do not own cars [are in **developing** world and
**crave**]{.underline} the kind of [personal **mobility**]{.underline}

will require them to remain confined forever or to rely solely on

### Economy\-\--AT: Lithium Batteries\-\--1NC

#### No lithium shortages -- African Markets, Consumer Preferences, and Market Slowdown Solves

**Homes**, Andy 7-11-**24** Senior metals columnist who previously
covered industrial metals markets for Metals Week and was EMEA
commodities editor at Knight-Ridder (subsequently Bridge). Started up
Metals Insider in 2003 and sold it to Thomson Reuters in 2008, he is
author of 'Siberian Dreams' (2006) about the Russian Arctic (After
another boom and bust, where next for lithium? \| reuters. (n.d.).
https://www.reuters.com/markets/commodities/after-another-boom-bust-where-next-lithium-andy-home-2024-07-11/)

LONDON, July 11 (Reuters) - [Lithium boom has turned to lithium bust
over the last two years as a wave of **new supply** overwhelms
**weaker-than-expected** demand for electric vehicle (EV)
batteries]{.underline}.

The CME contract for lithium hydroxide has collapsed from a 2022 high of
\$85,000 per metric ton to \$11,930. The CME carbonate contract was
above \$40,000 when it began trading in July 2023 and has since slumped
to \$12,850.

[Lithium has **been here before**. There was a similar boom-bust cycle
in 2016-2017 but the difference this time is that no-one seems to be
expecting a speedy recovery.]{.underline}

The [short-term outlook is for]{.underline} [prices to trundle along at
the lows as the market digests surplus material]{.underline}.

The longer-term picture is more positive as governments force the
transition to electric vehicles but there will be no return to the giddy
heights of 2022 in the next 10 years, according to analysts at BMI, a
Fitch Solutions company.

Don\'t be too quick to write off either lithium or the electric vehicle
sector, though. The current price bust has been exacerbated by one-offs
on both the supply and demand sides of the equation.

[TOO MUCH SUPPLY]{.underline}

Lithium producers view their product as a bespoke chemical tailored to
battery-makers\' tight specifications rather than as a generic
commodity.

[Yet lithium\'s price behaviour is that of any other commodity, periods
of **high pricing encouraging** over-production which must then be
weeded out by periods of low pricing.]{.underline}

That pattern is currently playing out as producers trim output and defer
expansion plans in reaction to the new much lower price reality.

One little-discussed element of the current glut, however, is the surge
in artisanal mining (ASM) in Africa, particularly in Nigeria and
Zimbabwe.

[Research house CRU estimates that artisanal miners accounted for almost
two-thirds of African lithium supply in 2023 with volumes nearly
equivalent to the global market surplus last year.]{.underline}

African shipments of ore and low-grade concentrates accounted for a
quarter of China\'s total lithium imports in the first quarter of this
year on a metal contained basis, according to CRU.

Chinese entities are heavily involved in formalising artisanal supply
which is centred on previous tin and tantalum mining areas.

However, ASM is particularly price sensitive. The current wave of
independent production began when the price of spodumene ore was still
above \$6,000 per ton at the start of 2023. It is now closer to \$1,000,
challenging the economic viability of all but the richest of deposits.

More formalisation may result but as the cobalt market has learnt, ASM
can be a very significant and very fast-moving swing component of the
overall supply picture.

[TOO LITTLE DEMAND]{.underline}

Compounding lithium\'s price weakness has been a downgrade in
expectations for EV sales as the Chinese market matures and the Western
market loses some of its recent momentum.

[The EV revolution has hit a **slow patch** but is far from going into
reverse.]{.underline} BNP Paribas, for example, is still expecting
global sales growth of 23% this year, equivalent to over 18.7 million
vehicles.

[What has changed, however, is the product mix. Sales of pure battery
vehicles are **flat-lining**, while sales of hybrid gas-electric
vehicles are booming, even in China.]{.underline}

[Chinese sales of plug-in hybrids jumped by 90% year-on-year in April
and May, while those of pure electric vehicles rose by a comparatively
modest 10%, according to BNP.]{.underline}

This may be good news for the broader energy transition but it\'s not so
good for lithium since many hybrids use a nickel hydride battery with no
lithium, the bank notes.

Hybrids, once viewed as a mere stepping stone on the journey from
internal combustion to fully electric engines are proving remarkably
popular with consumers worried about charging infrastructure and the
relatively high cost of pure battery vehicles.

Automakers are responding. Stellantis [(STLAM.MI), opens new
tab](https://www.reuters.com/markets/companies/STLAM.MI), which saw
hybrid sales grow by 41% year-on-year in Europe in the first half of the
year, plans [to
expand](https://www.reuters.com/business/autos-transportation/stellantis-expand-hybrid-vehicle-line-meet-growing-demand-2024-07-09/) its
line of affordable hybrid vehicles to 36 models in Europe by 2026.

So too are governments. [The Joe Biden administration has [rowed
back](https://www.reuters.com/business/autos-transportation/plug-in-polluters-how-bidens-emissions-rules-go-soft-hybrid-trucks-suvs-2024-06-06/) on
its target of converting two-thirds of new vehicles to battery electric
vehicles by 2032, allowing auto-makers to step up production of hybrids
instead.]{.underline}

[FULL CHARGE AHEAD]{.underline}

[The automotive energy transition is still in full swing but is not
matching the exuberant forecasts made a couple of years
ago.]{.underline}

There is now too much lithium supply and too much battery capacity
relative to consumer demand for electric vehicles.

The slowdown could easily reverse.

[One critical facilitator will be the build-out of EV **charging
infrastructure**, a sector which has attracted **far less investment**
than battery manufacturing.]{.underline}

Car buyers in Europe and the United States are choosing hybrids because
of range anxiety and they will continue to do so until they see more
charging points.

[The second key factor is price, with electric vehicles **still more
expensive** than traditional cars everywhere outside of
China.]{.underline}

[Just as high lithium prices were]{.underline} [good news for
**producers** but not **battery-makers**]{.underline}, current low
prices are bad news for the lithium sector but very good news for
consumers in the form of lower battery prices.

[Lithium-ion battery packs registered a 7% increase in price between
2021 and 2022, breaking a long-running downtrend, according to the
International Energy Agency. High lithium prices were matched by bull
markets in other battery inputs such as cobalt, nickel and
graphite.]{.underline}

[Battery prices are now tumbling as prices for lithium and other
materials fall.]{.underline}

[The average Asian nickel-cobalt-manganese battery cell price fell to
\$90 per kilowatt hour in May, according to analysts at Benchmark
Mineral Intelligence. That was the lowest since 2021 and a long way off
the March 2022 peak of \$166.]{.underline}

### Economy\-\--AT: Overfishing\-\--1NC

#### [No overfishing]{.underline}- stocks increasing, [self-interest]{.underline} checks, alternatives worse

**Hillborn, 19** \-- University of Washington aquatic and fishery
science professor \[Ray, and Ulrike Hilborn, Ocean Recovery: A
sustainable future for global fisheries?, 2019, accessed 12-14-19,
language corrected\]

Unnoticed in all this frenzy of doom, [a stubbornly persistent
cornucopia of offerings at the fish counters]{.underline} of the Western
world [has remained the same]{.underline}, has [possibly increased
even]{.underline}. [There has been no falling off in
bounty.]{.underline}

It should come as no surprise that the real situation is complex: there
are failures and successes. Even though there have been no fish
extinctions, there have been major fish stock collapses, but there have
also been recoveries. [The ocean, far from being drained of them, still
contains lots of fish]{.underline}, [perhaps as many as there were
before industrial fishing began.]{.underline} It is almost perverse that
it is precisely in most of the doom-laden developed countries that [fish
stocks are **not even the tiniest bit declining**, but
**increasing**]{.underline}. [The disconnect between the well-stocked
seafood counters and the common narrative of gloom]{.underline} and doom
[is truly perplexing.]{.underline}

(p.vi) I recognize that most people find themselves outside the world of
fisheries management and are not familiar with the great complexities of
past and present developments. I also recognize that the ever-growing
pressure from advocacy groups can easily take us in the wrong direction,
away from solutions that have been shown to work. Hence, this book.

My aim is to provide interested readers outside of fisheries science
with an overview of the major issues associated with managing global
fisheries, but also with enough detail that anyone can dig into specific
topics. My hope is to correct many of the most common misconceptions
about how fisheries are managed, the status and sustainability of fish
stocks, and the relative environmental cost of catching fish in the
ocean compared with producing food from the land.

Some teasers that may surprise you and encourage you to read more.

[In most of the developed world, the abundance of fish is increasing not
decreasing]{.underline}. [Overall, global fish abundance is]{.underline}
probably [stable]{.underline}.

[The number of fish in the ocean now is likely higher than it was before
industrial fishing began]{.underline}, [and their total weight may be
close to what it was before large-scale fishing]{.underline}. (Hint:
Many fisheries target predators; therefore, more prey survive. Smaller
fish in greater numbers equals more total weight.)

Overfishing can be sustainable.

[There is **no widespread extinction** of marine fish]{.underline}.
[Only one marine fish has been documented to go extinct, and it was not
due to fishing.]{.underline}

Marine Protected Areas (MPAs) will only increase the abundance of fish
where fish stocks are seriously overfished.

[Commercial fisher\[s\]]{.underline}~~men~~ [have more of a **financial
interest** in sustainability than NGOs]{.underline} or academics like
me. A collapse puts fishermen out of business, whereas NGOs and
academics see their funding increase.

[Capture fisheries are among the **lowest environmental impact** forms
of food production]{.underline}. A thoughtful piscivore could have a
lower environmental impact than that of a vegan.

### Economy\-\--AT: Ozone\-\--1NC

#### No ozone impact.

Dr. Vadim **Gordienko 22**. Ph.D.; Head of Tectonosphere Department,
S.I.Subbotine Institute of Geophysics. "Ozone and ultraviolet in the
Earth\'s atmosphere." Geology and Mineral Resources of World Ocean,
Volume 18, Issue 2.

The appearance of the ozone layer (OL) in the Earth's atmosphere and the
associated processes of attenuation of the ultraviolet (UV) part of
solar radiation reaching the surface are considered. It is shown that
the [[role of OL in protecting]{.mark} **living [organisms]{.mark}**
from the **destructive effects** of exposure to the short-wavelength
part of the insolation spectrum [is **immensely
exaggerated**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Ozone is generated by UV radiation
below the ionosphere when oxygen molecules in the air are divided. When
absorbed by UV rays, it is destroyed. Ozone exists for the longest time
at minimum temperatures of the atmosphere. This circumstance and the
[[**increased density** lead to]{.underline}]{.mark} the
[[accumulation]{.mark} of O3 [above the tropopause]{.mark}]{.underline},
where the OL is formed. The [amount of **simultaneously existing** gas
is **extremely insignificant**]{.underline} --- [about **10⁻⁶
air**]{.underline}. It is this circumstance that makes it possible to
[[estimate its contribution to]{.mark} the [absorption of **UV**]{.mark}
**rays** as **[vanishingly small]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Compared with
it, the [**[scattering]{.mark} of rays** [by **air**]{.mark}
**molecules** [is]{.mark}]{.underline} incomparably [[more
effective]{.underline}]{.mark}, although each single act of absorption
of the middle part of the ultraviolet is several times greater than a
single result of scattering. One can agree with the idea of the
elimination of ozone molecules by reaction with freons and other gases
containing halogens, including those of volcanic origin. It is also
possible that it interacts with hydrogen, but H2 rather arising in the
atmosphere, and not in the bowels of the Earth. But the [processes of
**changing the OL**]{.underline}, [including]{.underline} the
[**periodic formation** of]{.underline} «**[[ozone
holes]{.underline}]{.mark}**», [[do not pose **any danger** and are
explained by **natural phenomena**]{.underline}]{.mark}, and
[[not]{.underline}]{.mark} by [[human activity]{.underline}]{.mark}. A
very expensive campaign to save the layer makes no sense. On the other
hand, ground5level ozone concentration increases over time precisely
under the influence of industrial emissions. In the cities of developed
countries, the content of this poisonous gas already often exceeds
permissible levels and it is time to take up more effective
counteraction to its accumulation.

## AT: Arctic Sustainability ADV

### AT: Arctic Sustainability\-\--1NC

#### [Best research]{.underline} shows ice is [stable]{.underline}\-\--no [tipping points]{.underline} or [collapse]{.underline}.

**Bastach '15** \[Michael; 2011; Senior Reporter with The Daily Caller
News Foundation, a D.C.-based News Organization Specializing in Policy
Reporting and Investigative Journalism; Daily Caller, "Irreversible'
Arctic Ice Loss Seems to Be Reversing Itself,"
http://dailycaller.com/2015/04/29/irreversible-arctic-ice-loss-seems-to-be-reversing-itself/#ixzz4LO47rQRG\]

For years, [scientists have been warning the Arctic was in a "death
spiral]{.underline}" and could soon be ice-free during the summertime
and shrink to unprecedented levels due to man-made global warming. Such
ice loss could be "irreversible," some scientists claimed.

But [**[new research]{.mark}** from the Scripps Institution of
Oceanography [says]{.mark} that [predictions of]{.mark} a permanently
[**ice-free** Arctic are]{.mark} based on **["oversimplified"]{.mark}**
theories]{.underline}. [Scripps researchers,]{.underline} who were
co-funded by the Navy, [found that the Arctic sea [ice may be
"substantially **more stable** than]{.mark} has been [suggested]{.mark}
in previous idealized modeling studies]{.underline}."

"We found that [two [key]{.mark} physical [processes]{.mark}, which
[were]{.mark}]{.underline} often **[[overlooked]{.underline}]{.mark}**
in previous process models, [were]{.underline} actually
[essential]{.underline} for accurately describing whether sea ice loss
is reversible," Scripps climate scientist Ian Eisenman, co-author of a
new study refuting claims the Arctic is in a "death spiral," said in a
statement.

"Our results show that [the basis for a sea ice **tipping point**
doesn't hold up when these additional processes are
considered]{.underline}," echoed Till Wagner, also a Scripps scientist.
"In other words, [[**no tipping point** is]{.mark} likely [to
devour]{.mark} what's left of the [Arctic]{.mark} summer sea
[ice]{.mark}]{.underline}. So if global warming does soon melt all the
Arctic sea ice, at least we can expect to get it back if we somehow
manage to cool the planet back down again."

"If the associated parameters are set to values that correspond to the
current climate, the ice retreat is reversible and there is no
instability when the climate is warmed," according to Eisenman and
Wagner's study.

Eisenman and Wagner's study comes after the Arctic hit its lowest
maximum sea ice extent on record during February. This was followed by
the Arctic having its lowest ice extent for March on record, according
to the National Snow and Ice Data Center. Indeed, Arctic sea ice has
been declining at a rate of 2.6 percent per decade since 1979.

Scientists and climate pundits have already predicted this year's Arctic
summer sea ice extent will be the lowest on record, following poor
winter extent. This has only bolstered claims that the Arctic could soon
be ice free.

"Summertime Arctic sea ice is not long for this world," lamented Joe
Romm, a climate scientist and editor for the liberal blog ThinkProgress.
"Because of Arctic amplification, the Arctic warms twice as fast (or
more) than the Earth as a whole does."

Romm goes on to cite a February study claiming that Arctic ice is losing
its thickness and becoming more susceptible to warmer weather ---
meaning it's melting a lot faster. The study, published in the journal
The Cryosphere found that "annual mean ice thickness has decreased from
3.59 meters \[11.8 feet\] in 1975 to 1.25 m \[4.1 feet\] in 2012, a 65%
reduction."

"The ice is thinning dramatically," climatologist Ron Lindsay, the
study's lead author, was quoted saying.

It wasn't long ago that David Barber, Canada's Research Chair in Arctic
System Science at the University of Manitoba, warned there was almost no
multi-year ice left in the Northern Hemisphere.

"We are almost out of multiyear sea ice in the northern hemisphere," he
told Canada's Parliament in 2009. "I've never seen anything like this in
my 30 years of working in the high Arctic ... it was very dramatic."

Arctic sea ice extent that year was at its third-lowest extent on
record, behind 2007 and 2008, and experts were saying there would be no
polar ice during the summer by 2030 for the first time in one million
years.

"I would argue that, from a practical perspective, we almost have a
seasonally ice-free Arctic now, because multiyear sea ice is the barrier
to the use and development of the Arctic," Barber said.

But such [[predictions]{.mark} have **[fall]{.mark}**en [flat,]{.mark}
as the [Arctic has seen a **resurgence**]{.mark} of multi-year ice since
2009]{.underline}.

NSIDC and European satellite data show that multi-year sea ice made a
big comeback in 2013 and 2014 --- increasing from 2.25 to 3.17 million
square kilometers during that time and making up 43 percent of the north
pole's ice pack.

In fact, [Arctic sea [ice]{.mark} extent [**as a whole** seems to be
**stabilizing**]{.mark} despite this year's record low
maximum]{.underline} in February. NSIDC data shows [Arctic sea ice
extent is currently [**within** the **normal
range**]{.mark}]{.underline} based on the 1981 to 2010 average extent.

["Global sea ice is [at a record high, **a**]{.mark}nother [key
**indicator**]{.mark} that [something is]{.mark} working in the
**[opposite]{.mark}** direction of what was predicted,"]{.underline} Dr.
Benny Peiser, [director of the Global Warming Policy Forum, told the
U.K. Express]{.underline} in January.

"Most [[people think]{.mark} the [poles are melting... **they're
not,"**]{.mark}]{.underline} he said. "This is a huge inconvenience that
[reality is now catching up with climate alarmists]{.underline}, who
were predicting that the poles would be melting fairly soon."

### AT: Arctic Sustainability\-\--2NC

#### [Adaptation]{.underline} is [guaranteed]{.underline}, [zeroing]{.underline} the impact.

**Lomborg '21** \[Bjorn; 2021; President of the Copenhagen Consensus
Center, Former Director of the Danish Government\'s Environmental
Assessment Institute, PhD in Political Science at the University of
Copenhagen, M.A. in Political Science at the University of Aarhus, BA
from the University of Georgia; Wall Street Journal, "Climate Change
Calls for Adaptation, Not Panic,"
https://www.wsj.com/articles/climate-change-adaptation-panic-exaggerating-disaster-11634760376\]

[It's easy [to construct **climate disasters**. You]{.mark} just [find
a]{.mark} current, disconcerting [trend and **project it**]{.mark} into
the future, while [**ignoring** everything]{.mark} humanity could do [to
**adapt**]{.mark}. For instance]{.underline}, one widely reported study
found that **[heat waves]{.underline}** could kill thousands more
Americans by the end of the century if global warming continues
apace---but only if you assume people won't use more air conditioning.
[Yes, the [climate is]{.mark} likely [to change, but **so is
human**]{.mark} **behavior** in **[response]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

[Adaptation doesn't make the cost of global warming go away entirely,
but it does **reduce it dramatically**]{.underline}. Higher temperatures
will shrink harvests if [farmers]{.underline} keep growing the same
crops, but [they're likely to **adapt** by growing other varieties or
different plants altogether]{.underline}. Corn [production]{.underline}
in North America has **[shift]{.underline}**ed away from the Southeast
toward the Upper Midwest, where farmers take advantage of longer growing
seasons and less-frequent extreme heat. [[When sea levels rise,
governments build **defenses**]{.mark}---like the levees, flood walls
and drainage systems]{.underline} that protected New Orleans from much
of Hurricane Ida's ferocity this year.

[Nonetheless, many]{.underline} in the media [push **unrealistic
projections** of climate catastrophes, while **ignoring** adaptation. A
new study documents how the **biggest [bias]{.mark}** [in
studies]{.mark} on the rise of sea levels [is]{.mark} their [tendency to
ignore]{.mark} human [adaptation, **exaggerating**]{.mark} flood
[risks]{.mark}]{.underline} in 2100 [by as much as **[1,300
times]{.mark}**]{.underline}. It is also evident in the breathless tone
of most reporting: The Washington Post frets that sea level rise could
"make 187 million people homeless," CNN fears an "underwater future,"
and USA Today agonizes over tens of trillions of dollars in projected
annual flood damage. All three rely on [studies]{.underline} that
[**implausibly** assume no society]{.underline} across the world [will
make]{.underline} any [adaptation]{.underline} whatever for the rest of
the century. [This isn't **reporting** but
**scaremongering**]{.underline}.

You can see how far from reality these sorts of projections are in one
heavily cited study, depicted in the graph nearby If you assume no
society will adapt to any sea-level rise between now and 2100, you'll
find that vast areas of the world will be routinely flooded, causing
\$55 trillion in damage annually in 2100 (expressed in 2005 dollars), or
about 5% of global gross domestic product. But as the study emphasizes,
[["in **reality**]{.mark}, societies are **likely** to
adapt."]{.underline}

By raising the height of dikes, the study shows that [[humanity]{.mark}
can **[negate]{.mark}** almost [**all** that]{.mark} terrible
**projected [damage]{.mark}**]{.underline} by 2100. Only 15,000 people
would be flooded every year, which is a remarkable improvement compared
with the 3.4 million people flooded in 2000. The total cost of damage,
investments in new dikes, and maintenance costs of existing dikes will
fall sixfold between now and 2100 to 0.008% of world GDP.

Adaptation is much more effective than climate regulations at staving
off flood risks. Compare the two types of policies in isolation. Without
any climate mitigation to help, dikes would still safeguard more than
99.99% of the flood victims you'd see if global warming continued on
current trends. Instead of 187 million people flooded in 2100, there
would be only 15,000. Climate policy achieves much less on its own.
Without adaptation, even stringent regulations that keep the global
temperature rise below 2 degrees Celsius would reduce the number of
flood victims only down to 85 million a year by the end of the century.

Stringent climate policy still has only a mild effect when used in
concert with dikes: Instead of the 15,000 flood victims you'd get with
only adaptation, you'd have 10,000. And getting there would cost
hundreds of trillions of dollars, which is hardly mitigated by the \$40
billion drop in total flood damage and dike costs climate regulations
would achieve. As I've explained in these pages before, this kind of
policy has a high human cost: the tens of millions of people pricey
climate regulations relegate to poverty.

You don't have to portend doom to take climate change seriously.
[[Ignoring]{.mark} the benefits of [adaptation]{.mark} may make for
better headlines, but it **[badly misinforms]{.mark}**
readers]{.underline}.

[As nuclear projects advance]{.underline} in Canada, [[it\'s vital
to]{.mark} [respect]{.mark} [I]{.mark}]{.underline}ndigenous
